mw-hide-empty-elt ns-0 ns-subject mw-editable page-Human_evolution rootpage-Human_evolution skin-vector action-view"> <div class="noprint" id="mw-page-base"></div>\n<div class="noprint" id="mw-head-base"></div>\n<div class="mw-body" id="content" role="main">\n<a id="top"></a>\n<div class="mw-body-content" id="siteNotice"><!-- CentralNotice --></div><div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Human evolution</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Evolutionary process leading up to the appearance of anatomically modern humans</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Akha_cropped.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="484" data-file-width="304" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Akha_cropped.png/170px-Akha_cropped.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Akha_cropped.png/255px-Akha_cropped.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c5/Akha_cropped.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Akha_cropped.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i></div></div></div><p><b>Human evolution</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> process that led to the emergence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a>, beginning with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primate_evolution" title="Primate evolution">evolutionary history</a> of <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a>&#8212;in particular <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i>&#8212;and leading to the emergence of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens</a></i> as a distinct species of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominid" title="Hominid">hominid family</a>, the great apes. This process involved the gradual development of traits such as  <a href="/wiki/Human_skeletal_changes_due_to_bipedalism" title="Human skeletal changes due to bipedalism">human bipedalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HallHallgr&#237;msson2011c_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-HallHallgr&#237;msson2011c-1">[1]</a></sup> as well as interbreeding with other <a href="/wiki/Hominini" title="Hominini">hominins</a>, which indicate that human evolution was not linear but a web.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ackermann_2015_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ackermann_2015-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denisovans_&amp;_Neandertals_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Denisovans_&amp;_Neandertals-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Hybrids_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Hybrids-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosaic_humans,_the_hybrid_species_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mosaic_humans,_the_hybrid_species-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The study of human evolution involves several scientific disciplines, including <a href="/wiki/Biological_anthropology" title="Biological anthropology">physical anthropology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Primatology" title="Primatology">primatology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paleontology" title="Paleontology">paleontology</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neurobiology" title="Neurobiology">neurobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethology" title="Ethology">ethology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology" title="Evolutionary psychology">evolutionary psychology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Embryology" title="Embryology">embryology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Genetic studies show that primates diverged from other <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=85">85</a>&#160;million years ago</span>, in the <a href="/wiki/Late_Cretaceous" title="Late Cretaceous">Late Cretaceous</a> period, and the earliest <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> appear in the <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a>, around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=55">55</a>&#160;million years ago</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominoidea" title="Hominoidea">Hominoidea</a> (apes) superfamily, the <a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae">Hominidae</a> <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> diverged from the <a href="/wiki/Gibbon" title="Gibbon">Hylobatidae</a> (gibbon) family some 15&#8211;20 million years ago; African great apes (subfamily <a href="/wiki/Homininae" title="Homininae">Homininae</a>) diverged from <a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">orangutans</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ponginae" title="Ponginae">Ponginae</a>) about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=14">14</a>&#160;million years ago</span>; the <a href="/wiki/Hominini" title="Hominini">Hominini</a> tribe (humans, <i>Australopithecines</i> and other extinct biped genera, and <a href="/wiki/Pan_(genus)" title="Pan (genus)">chimpanzee</a>) parted from the <a href="/wiki/Gorillini" title="Gorillini">Gorillini</a> tribe (gorillas) between 8&#8211;9 million years ago; and, in turn, the subtribes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hominina" title="Hominina">Hominina</a> (humans and biped ancestors) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panina" title="Panina">Panina</a> (chimps) separated 4&#8211;7.5 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Max_Planck_2012_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Max_Planck_2012-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" class="mw-headline" id="Anatomical_changes">Anatomical changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Anatomical changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ape_skeletons.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="588" data-file-width="1081" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Ape_skeletons.png/350px-Ape_skeletons.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Ape_skeletons.png/525px-Ape_skeletons.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Ape_skeletons.png/700px-Ape_skeletons.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ape_skeletons.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ape" title="Ape">hominoids</a> are descendants of a <a href="/wiki/Common_descent" title="Common descent">common ancestor</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Human evolution from its first separation from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chimpanzee-human_last_common_ancestor" title="Chimpanzee-human last common ancestor">last common ancestor of humans and chimpanzees</a> is characterized by a number of <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_development_(biology)" title="Human development (biology)">developmental</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_physiology" title="Human physiology">physiological</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Human_behavior" title="Human behavior">behavioral</a> changes.\nThe most significant of these adaptations are bipedalism, increased brain size, lengthened <a href="/wiki/Ontogeny" title="Ontogeny">ontogeny</a> (gestation and infancy), and decreased <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>. The relationship between these changes is the subject of ongoing debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (February 2015)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other significant morphological changes included the evolution of a <a href="/wiki/Thumb#Grips" title="Thumb">power and precision grip</a>, a change first occurring in <i>H. erectus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bipedalism">Bipedalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Bipedalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipedal" title="Bipedal">Bipedalism</a> is the basic adaptation of the hominid and is considered the main cause behind a suite of skeletal changes shared by all bipedal hominids. The earliest hominin, of presumably primitive bipedalism, is considered to be either <i><a href="/wiki/Sahelanthropus" title="Sahelanthropus">Sahelanthropus</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brunet2002_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brunet2002-11">[11]</a></sup> or <i><a href="/wiki/Orrorin" title="Orrorin">Orrorin</a></i>, both of which arose some 6 to 7 million years ago. The non-bipedal knuckle-walkers, the gorilla and chimpanzee, diverged from the hominin line over a period covering the same time, so either of <i>Sahelanthropus</i> or <i>Orrorin</i> may be our <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee%E2%80%93human_last_common_ancestor" title="Chimpanzee&#8211;human last common ancestor">last shared ancestor</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Ardipithecus" title="Ardipithecus">Ardipithecus</a></i>, a full biped, arose somewhat later.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The early bipeds eventually evolved into the australopithecines and still later into the genus <i>Homo</i>. There are several theories of the adaptation value of bipedalism. It is possible that bipedalism was favored because it freed the hands for reaching and carrying food, saved energy during locomotion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwang_Hyun_2015_929&#8211;934_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kwang_Hyun_2015_929&#8211;934-12">[12]</a></sup> enabled long distance running and hunting, provided an enhanced field of vision, and helped avoid hyperthermia by reducing the surface area exposed to direct sun; features all advantageous for thriving in the new savanna and woodland environment created as a result of the East African Rift Valley uplift versus the previous closed forest habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeSalleTattersall2008_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeSalleTattersall2008-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwang_Hyun_2015_929&#8211;934_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kwang_Hyun_2015_929&#8211;934-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curry2008_Child_of_Earth_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curry2008_Child_of_Earth-14">[14]</a></sup> A new study provides support for the hypothesis that walking on two legs, or bipedalism, evolved because it used less energy than quadrupedal knuckle-walking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> However, recent studies suggest that bipedality without the <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">ability to use fire</a> would not have allowed global dispersal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> This change in gait saw a lengthening of the legs proportionately when compared to the length of the arms, which were shortened through the removal of the need for <a href="/wiki/Brachiation" title="Brachiation">brachiation</a>.  Another change is the shape of the big toe.  Recent studies suggest that Australopithecines still lived part of the time in trees as a result of maintaining a grasping big toe.  This was progressively lost in Habilines.\n</p><p>Anatomically, the evolution of bipedalism has been accompanied by a large number of skeletal changes, not just to the legs and pelvis, but also to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_vertebral_column" title="Human vertebral column">vertebral column</a>, feet and ankles, and skull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Femur" title="Femur">femur</a> evolved into a slightly more angular position to move the center of gravity toward the geometric center of the body. The knee and ankle joints became increasingly robust to better support increased weight. To support the increased weight on each vertebra in the upright position, the human vertebral column became S-shaped and the <a href="/wiki/Lumbar_vertebrae" title="Lumbar vertebrae">lumbar vertebrae</a> became shorter and wider. In the feet the big toe moved into alignment with the other toes to help in forward locomotion. The arms and forearms shortened relative to the legs making it easier to run. The foramen magnum migrated under the skull and more anterior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The most significant changes occurred in the pelvic region, where the long downward facing <a href="/wiki/Ilium_(bone)" title="Ilium (bone)">iliac blade</a> was shortened and widened as a requirement for keeping the center of gravity stable while walking;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Srivastava2009_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Srivastava2009-20">[20]</a></sup> bipedal hominids have a shorter but broader, bowl-like pelvis due to this. A drawback is that the birth canal of bipedal apes is smaller than in knuckle-walking apes, though there has been a widening of it in comparison to that of australopithecine and modern humans, permitting the passage of newborns due to the increase in cranial size but this is limited to the upper portion, since further increase can hinder normal bipedal movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The shortening of the pelvis and smaller birth canal evolved as a requirement for bipedalism and had significant effects on the process of human birth which is much more difficult in modern humans than in other primates. During human birth, because of the variation in size of the pelvic region, the fetal head must be in a transverse position (compared to the mother) during entry into the birth canal and rotate about 90 degrees upon exit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The smaller birth canal became a limiting factor to brain size increases in early humans and prompted a shorter gestation period leading to the relative immaturity of human offspring, who are unable to walk much before 12 months and have greater <a href="/wiki/Neoteny" title="Neoteny">neoteny</a>, compared to other primates, who are mobile at a much earlier age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curry2008_Child_of_Earth_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Curry2008_Child_of_Earth-14">[14]</a></sup> The increased brain growth after birth and the increased dependency of children on mothers had a big effect upon the female reproductive cycle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>  and the more frequent appearance of <a href="/wiki/Alloparenting" title="Alloparenting">alloparenting</a> in humans when compared with other hominids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Delayed human sexual maturity also led to the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">menopause</a> with one explanation providing that elderly women could better pass on their genes by taking care of their daughter\'s offspring, as compared to having more children of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wayman_Sciencenews_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wayman_Sciencenews-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Encephalization">Encephalization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Encephalization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2040" data-file-width="2750" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg/370px-Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg/555px-Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg/740px-Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg 2x" width="370"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Brain size and tooth size in hominins</div></div></div>\n<p>The human species eventually developed a much larger brain than that of other primates&#8212;typically 1,330&#160;cm<sup>3</sup> (81&#160;cu&#160;in) in modern humans, nearly three times the size of a chimpanzee or gorilla brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schoeneman_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schoeneman-26">[26]</a></sup> After a period of stasis with <i>Australopithecus anamensis</i> and <i>Ardipithecus</i>, species which had smaller brains as a result of their bipedal locomotion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> the pattern of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encephalization" title="Encephalization">encephalization</a> started with <i>Homo habilis</i>, whose 600&#160;cm<sup>3</sup> (37&#160;cu&#160;in) brain was slightly larger than that of chimpanzees. This evolution continued in <i>Homo erectus</i> with 800&#8211;1,100&#160;cm<sup>3</sup> (49&#8211;67&#160;cu&#160;in), and reached a maximum in Neanderthals with 1,200&#8211;1,900&#160;cm<sup>3</sup> (73&#8211;116&#160;cu&#160;in), larger even than modern <i>Homo sapiens</i>. This brain increase manifested during postnatal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_development" title="Neural development">brain growth</a>, far exceeding that of other apes (<a href="/wiki/Heterochrony" title="Heterochrony">heterochrony</a>).  It also allowed for extended periods of <a href="/wiki/Observational_learning" title="Observational learning">social learning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Language_acquisition" title="Language acquisition">language acquisition</a> in juvenile humans, beginning as much as 2 million years ago.\n</p><p>Furthermore, the changes in the structure of <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brains</a> may be even more significant than the increase in size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Park2007_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Park2007-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bruner2007_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bruner2007-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard_2007_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard_2007-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg"><img alt="Three students hold three different skulls in front of their faces, to show the difference in size and shape compared to the modern head" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1765" data-file-width="2017" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg/220px-Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg/330px-Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg/440px-Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The size and shape of the skull changed over time.  The leftmost, and largest, is a replica of a modern human skull.</div></div></div><p> The <a href="/wiki/Temporal_lobe" title="Temporal lobe">temporal lobes</a>, which contain centers for language processing, have increased disproportionately, as has the <a href="/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex" title="Prefrontal cortex">prefrontal cortex</a>, which has been related to complex decision-making and moderating social behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schoeneman_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schoeneman-26">[26]</a></sup> Encephalization has been tied to increased meat and starches in the diet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150813_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150813-34">[34]</a></sup> and the development of cooking,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Organ_2011_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Organ_2011-35">[35]</a></sup> and it has been proposed that intelligence increased as a response to an increased necessity for <a href="/wiki/Dunbar%27s_number" title="Dunbar\'s number">solving social problems</a> as human society became more complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Changes in skull morphology, such as smaller mandibles and mandible muscle attachments, allowed more room for the brain to grow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The increase in volume of the <a href="/wiki/Neocortex" title="Neocortex">neocortex</a> also included a rapid increase in size of the <a href="/wiki/Cerebellum" title="Cerebellum">cerebellum</a>. Its function has traditionally been associated with balance and fine motor control, but more recently with <a href="/wiki/Speech" title="Speech">speech</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a>. The great apes, including hominids, had a more pronounced cerebellum relative to the neocortex than other primates. It has been suggested that because of its function of sensory-motor control and learning complex muscular actions, the cerebellum may have underpinned human technological adaptations, including the preconditions of speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The immediate survival advantage of encephalization is difficult to discern, as the major brain changes from <i>Homo erectus</i> to <i>Homo heidelbergensis</i> were not accompanied by major changes in technology.  It has been suggested that the changes were mainly social and behavioural, including increased empathic abilities,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> increases in size of social groups,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> and increased behavioural plasticity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Encephalization may be due to a dependency on calorie dense difficult to acquire food <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_dimorphism">Sexual dimorphism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Sexual dimorphism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The reduced degree of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism_in_humans" title="Sexual dimorphism in humans">sexual dimorphism in humans</a> is visible primarily in the reduction of the male <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine tooth</a> relative to other ape species (except <a href="/wiki/Gibbon" title="Gibbon">gibbons</a>) and reduced brow ridges and general robustness of males. Another important physiological change related to sexuality in humans was the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Concealed_ovulation" title="Concealed ovulation">hidden estrus</a>. Humans are the only hominoids in which the female is fertile year round and in which no special signals of fertility are produced by the body (such as genital swelling or overt changes in proceptivity during estrus).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nonetheless, humans retain a degree of sexual dimorphism in the distribution of body hair and subcutaneous fat, and in the overall size, males being around 15% larger than females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> These changes taken together have been interpreted as a result of an increased emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Pair_bond" title="Pair bond">pair bonding</a> as a possible solution to the requirement for increased parental investment due to the prolonged infancy of offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ulnar_opposition">Ulnar opposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ulnar opposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The ulnar opposition&#8212;the contact between the <a href="/wiki/Thumb" title="Thumb">thumb</a> and the tip of the <a href="/wiki/Little_finger" title="Little finger">little finger</a> of the same hand&#8212;is unique to the <a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">genus Homo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> including Neanderthals, the <a href="/wiki/Atapuerca_Mountains" title="Atapuerca Mountains">Sima de los Huesos</a> <a href="/wiki/Hominini" title="Hominini">hominins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens">anatomically modern humans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miriam_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miriam-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In other primates the thumb is short and unable to touch the little finger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miriam_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Miriam-53">[53]</a></sup> The ulnar opposition facilitates the precision grip and power grip of the human hand, underlying all the skilled manipulations.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_changes">Other changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A number of other changes have also characterized the evolution of humans, among them an increased importance on vision rather than smell; a longer juvenile developmental period and higher infant dependency; a smaller gut; faster basal metabolism;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> loss of body hair; evolution of sweat glands; a change in the shape of the dental arcade from being u-shaped to being parabolic; development of a <a href="/wiki/Chin" title="Chin">chin</a> (found in <i>Homo sapiens</i> alone); development of <a href="/wiki/Temporal_styloid_process" title="Temporal styloid process">styloid processes</a>; and the development of a <a href="/wiki/Larynx#Descended_larynx" title="Larynx">descended larynx</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_study">History of study</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: History of study">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div style="float: right;">\n<div class="toccolours searchaux" id="Container" style="text-align: left; padding:0.5em 0 0 0; border-style:solid; float:right;clear:right; padding:0 0.5em; margin:0.3em 0 0.8em 1.4em; overflow: hidden;"><div id="Title" style="background-color:#77bb77; font-weight:bold; padding:0.2em; text-align:center; margin:0.6em 0 1.2em 0"><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_human_evolution#Hominini" title="Timeline of human evolution">Hominin timeline</a></div><div id="Contains-Timeline-Scale-Notes"><div style="text-align:right; position:relative; top:-1em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar"><span style="word-spacing:0">This box: </span><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Human_timeline" title="Template:Human timeline"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Human_timeline" title="Template talk:Human timeline"><span title="Discuss this template">talk</span></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Human_timeline&amp;action=edit"><span title="Edit this template">edit</span></a></li></ul></div></div><div id="Scale" style="width:3.1em; position:relative; top:-0.82em; float:left; height:36em; padding:0px"><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:36em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">-10&#160;&#8212;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:34.2em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">&#8211;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:32.4em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">-9&#160;&#8212;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:30.6em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">&#8211;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:28.8em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">-8&#160;&#8212;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:27em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">&#8211;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:25.2em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">-7&#160;&#8212;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:23.4em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">&#8211;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:21.6em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">-6&#160;&#8212;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:19.8em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">&#8211;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:18em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">-5&#160;&#8212;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:16.2em;"><span style="font-size:86%;white-space:nowrap;">&#8211;</span></div><div style="float:right; position:absolute; right:-1px; top:14.4em;"><span font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="position:relative; width:auto; z-index:10; font-size:90%; color:black; vertical-align:middle; line-height:100%; bottom:0;"></span></div></div><div class="annot-bar" style="width:auto; font-size:100%; position:absolute; text-align:center; margin-top:18em;"><div class="annot-nudge" style="float:left; position:relative; text-align:left; top:-0.75em; left:0em; width:7em;"><span style="clear:left;text-align:center;background-color:"><div style="float: right;font-size:85%;">(<a href="/wiki/Template:Hominins" title="Template:Hominins">view</a> &#8226; <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Hominins" title="Template talk:Hominins">discuss</a>)</div></div></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">Archaic humans</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_Darwin">Before Darwin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Before Darwin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The word <i>homo</i>, the name of the biological genus to which humans belong, is <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> for "human". It was chosen originally by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his classification system. The word "human" is from the Latin <i>humanus</i>, the adjectival form of <i>homo</i>. The Latin "homo" derives from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Indo-European</a> root *<i>dhghem</i>, or "earth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Linnaeus and other scientists of his time also considered the great apes to be the closest relatives of humans based on <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomical</a> similarities.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Darwin">Darwin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Darwin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The possibility of linking humans with earlier apes by descent became clear only after 1859 with the publication of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Origin_of_Species" title="On the Origin of Species">On the Origin of Species</a></i>, in which he argued for the idea of the evolution of new species from earlier ones. Darwin\'s book did not address the question of human evolution, saying only that "Light will be thrown on the origin of man and his history."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first debates about the nature of human evolution arose between <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Owen" title="Richard Owen">Richard Owen</a>. Huxley argued for human evolution from apes by illustrating many of the similarities and differences between humans and apes, and did so particularly in his 1863 book <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27s_Place_in_Nature" title="Man\'s Place in Nature">Evidence as to Man\'s Place in Nature</a></i>. However, many of Darwin\'s early supporters (such as <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace" title="Alfred Russel Wallace">Alfred Russel Wallace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lyell" title="Charles Lyell">Charles Lyell</a>) did not initially agree that the origin of the mental capacities and the moral sensibilities of humans could be explained by <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">natural selection</a>, though this later changed. Darwin applied the theory of evolution and <a href="/wiki/Sexual_selection" title="Sexual selection">sexual selection</a> to humans when he published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Descent_of_Man,_and_Selection_in_Relation_to_Sex" title="The Descent of Man, and Selection in Relation to Sex">The Descent of Man</a></i> in 1871.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_fossils">First fossils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: First fossils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>A major problem in the 19th century was the lack of <a href="/wiki/Transitional_fossil" title="Transitional fossil">fossil intermediaries</a>. Neanderthal remains were discovered in a limestone quarry in 1856, three years before the publication of <i>On the Origin of Species</i>, and Neanderthal fossils had been discovered in Gibraltar even earlier, but it was originally claimed that these were human remains of a creature suffering some kind of illness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Despite the 1891 discovery by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Dubois" title="Eug&#232;ne Dubois">Eug&#232;ne Dubois</a> of what is now called <i>Homo erectus</i> at <a href="/wiki/Trinil" title="Trinil">Trinil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, it was only in the 1920s when such fossils were discovered in Africa, that intermediate species began to accumulate.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 1925, <a href="/wiki/Raymond_Dart" title="Raymond Dart">Raymond Dart</a> described <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_africanus" title="Australopithecus africanus">Australopithecus africanus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Type_(biology)" title="Type (biology)">type specimen</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Taung_Child" title="Taung Child">Taung Child</a>, an australopithecine infant which was discovered in a cave. The child\'s remains were a remarkably well-preserved tiny skull and an <a href="/wiki/Endocast" title="Endocast">endocast</a> of the brain.\n</p><p>Although the brain was small (410&#160;cm<sup>3</sup>), its shape was rounded, unlike that of chimpanzees and <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorillas</a>, and more like a modern human brain. Also, the specimen showed short <a href="/wiki/Canine_tooth" title="Canine tooth">canine teeth</a>, and the position of the <a href="/wiki/Foramen_magnum" title="Foramen magnum">foramen magnum</a> (the hole in the skull where the spine enters) was evidence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipedal" title="Bipedal">bipedal</a> locomotion. All of these traits convinced Dart that the Taung Child was a bipedal human ancestor, a transitional form between apes and humans.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_East_African_fossils">The East African fossils</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The East African fossils">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fossil_hominids.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Fossil_hominids.jpg/150px-Fossil_hominids.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Fossil_hominids.jpg/225px-Fossil_hominids.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Fossil_hominids.jpg/300px-Fossil_hominids.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fossil_hominids.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">Fossil</a> <a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae">hominid</a> evolution display at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Museum_of_Osteology" title="The Museum of Osteology">The Museum of Osteology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma_City" title="Oklahoma City">Oklahoma City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">U.S.</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the 1960s and 1970s, hundreds of fossils were found in East Africa in the regions of the <a href="/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge" title="Olduvai Gorge">Olduvai Gorge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a>. The driving force of these searches was the Leakey family, with <a href="/wiki/Louis_Leakey" title="Louis Leakey">Louis Leakey</a> and his wife <a href="/wiki/Mary_Leakey" title="Mary Leakey">Mary Leakey</a>, and later their son <a href="/wiki/Richard_Leakey" title="Richard Leakey">Richard</a> and daughter-in-law <a href="/wiki/Meave_Leakey" title="Meave Leakey">Meave</a>&#8212;all  successful and world-renowned fossil hunters and <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">paleoanthropologists</a>. From the fossil beds of Olduvai and Lake Turkana they amassed specimens of the early hominins: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australopithecines" title="Australopithecines">australopithecines</a> and <i>Homo</i> species, and even <i>Homo erectus</i>.\n</p><p>These finds cemented Africa as the cradle of humankind. In the late 1970s and the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> emerged as the new hot spot of <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">paleoanthropology</a> after <a href="/wiki/Lucy_(Australopithecus)" title="Lucy (Australopithecus)">"Lucy"</a>, the most complete fossil member of the species <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Australopithecus afarensis</a></i>, was found in 1974 by <a href="/wiki/Donald_Johanson" title="Donald Johanson">Donald Johanson</a> near <a href="/wiki/Hadar,_Ethiopia" title="Hadar, Ethiopia">Hadar</a> in the desertic <a href="/wiki/Afar_Triangle" title="Afar Triangle">Afar Triangle</a> region of northern Ethiopia. Although the specimen had a small brain, the pelvis and leg bones were almost identical in function to those of modern humans, showing with certainty that these hominins had walked erect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Lucy was classified as a new species, <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Australopithecus afarensis</a></i>, which is thought to be more closely related to the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i> as a direct ancestor, or as a close relative of an unknown ancestor, than any other known hominid or hominin from this early time range; <i>see</i> <a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae">terms "hominid" and "hominin"</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> (The specimen was nicknamed "Lucy" after <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a>\' song "<a href="/wiki/Lucy_in_the_Sky_with_Diamonds" title="Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds">Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds</a>", which was played loudly and repeatedly in the camp during the excavations.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johanson_1981_22_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johanson_1981_22-63">[63]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Afar_Triangle" title="Afar Triangle">Afar Triangle</a> area would later yield discovery of many more hominin fossils, particularly those uncovered or described by teams headed by <a href="/wiki/Tim_D._White" title="Tim D. White">Tim D. White</a> in the 1990s, including <i><a href="/wiki/Ardipithecus#Ardipithecus_ramidus" title="Ardipithecus">Ardipithecus ramidus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ardipithecus#Ardipithecus_kadabba" title="Ardipithecus">Ardipithecus kadabba</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2013, fossil skeletons of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_naledi" title="Homo naledi">Homo naledi</a></i>, an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extinct_species" title="Extinct species">extinct species</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hominini" title="Hominini">hominin</a> assigned (provisionally) to the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i>, were found in the <a href="/wiki/Rising_Star_Cave" title="Rising Star Cave">Rising Star Cave</a> system, a site in <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_Humankind" title="Cradle of Humankind">Cradle of Humankind</a> region in <a href="/wiki/Gauteng" title="Gauteng">Gauteng</a> province near <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGS-20150910_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-NGS-20150910-66">[66]</a></sup> As of  September&#160;2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, fossils of at least fifteen individuals, amounting to 1,550 specimens, have been excavated from the cave.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NGS-20150910_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-NGS-20150910-66">[66]</a></sup> The species is characterized by a body mass and stature similar to small-bodied human populations, a smaller <a href="/wiki/Endocranium" title="Endocranium">endocranial</a> volume similar to <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a></i>, and a <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">cranial</a> <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> (skull shape) similar to early <i>Homo</i> species. The skeletal anatomy combines primitive features known from <a href="/wiki/Australopithecine" title="Australopithecine">australopithecines</a> with features known from early hominins. The individuals show signs of having been deliberately disposed of within the cave near the time of death. The fossils were dated close to 250,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and thus are not a direct ancestor but a contemporary with the first appearance of larger-brained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eLIFE-2017a_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-eLIFE-2017a-68">[68]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_genetic_revolution">The genetic revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: The genetic revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Leakey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="336" data-file-width="288" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Louis_Leakey.jpg/150px-Louis_Leakey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Louis_Leakey.jpg/225px-Louis_Leakey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fa/Louis_Leakey.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Leakey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Louis_Leakey" title="Louis Leakey">Louis Leakey</a> examining skulls from <a href="/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge" title="Olduvai Gorge">Olduvai Gorge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The genetic revolution in studies of human evolution started when <a href="/wiki/Vincent_Sarich" title="Vincent Sarich">Vincent Sarich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allan_Wilson" title="Allan Wilson">Allan Wilson</a> measured the strength of immunological cross-reactions of <a href="/wiki/Serum_(blood)" title="Serum (blood)">blood serum</a> <a href="/wiki/Albumin" title="Albumin">albumin</a> between pairs of creatures, including humans and African apes (chimpanzees and gorillas).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolClock_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-MolClock-69">[69]</a></sup> The strength of the reaction could be expressed numerically as an immunological distance, which was in turn proportional to the number of <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">amino acid</a> differences between homologous proteins in different species. By constructing a calibration curve of the ID of species\' pairs with known divergence times in the fossil record, the data could be used as a <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">molecular clock</a> to estimate the times of divergence of pairs with poorer or unknown fossil records.\n</p><p>In their seminal 1967 paper in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i>, Sarich and Wilson estimated the divergence time of humans and apes as four to five million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MolClock_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-MolClock-69">[69]</a></sup> at a time when standard interpretations of the fossil record gave this divergence as at least 10 to as much as 30 million years. Subsequent fossil discoveries, notably "Lucy", and reinterpretation of older fossil materials, notably <i><a href="/wiki/Sivapithecus" title="Sivapithecus">Ramapithecus</a></i>, showed the younger estimates to be correct and validated the albumin method.\n</p><p>Progress in <a href="/wiki/DNA_sequencing" title="DNA sequencing">DNA sequencing</a>, specifically <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (mtDNA) and then <a href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup">Y-chromosome DNA</a> (Y-DNA) advanced the understanding of human origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeSalleTattersall2008_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeSalleTattersall2008-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Application of the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">molecular clock</a> principle revolutionized the study of <a href="/wiki/Molecular_evolution" title="Molecular evolution">molecular evolution</a>.\n</p><p>On the basis of a separation from the orangutan between 10 and 20 million years ago, earlier studies of the molecular clock suggested that there were about 76 mutations per generation that were not inherited by human children from their parents; this evidence supported the divergence time between hominins and chimps noted above. However, a 2012 study in Iceland of 78 children and their parents suggests a mutation rate of only 36 mutations per generation; this datum extends the separation between humans and chimps to an earlier period greater than 7 million years ago (<a href="/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers" title="Year">Ma</a>). Additional research with 226 offspring of wild chimp populations in 8 locations suggests that chimps reproduce at age 26.5 years, on average; which suggests the human divergence from chimps occurred between 7 and 13 million years ago. And these data suggest that <i>Ardipithecus</i> (4.5 Ma), <i>Orrorin</i> (6 Ma) and <i>Sahelanthropus</i> (7 Ma) all may be on the hominid <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(evolution)" title="Lineage (evolution)">lineage</a>, and even that the separation may have occurred outside the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">East African Rift</a> region.\n</p><p>Furthermore, analysis of the two species\' genes in 2006 provides evidence that after human ancestors had started to diverge from chimpanzees, interspecies mating between "proto-human" and "proto-chimps" nonetheless occurred regularly enough to change certain genes in the new <a href="/wiki/Gene_pool" title="Gene pool">gene pool</a>:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>A new comparison of the human and chimp genomes suggests that after the two lineages separated, they may have begun interbreeding... A principal finding is that the <a href="/wiki/X_chromosome" title="X chromosome">X chromosomes</a> of humans and chimps appear to have <a href="/wiki/Genetic_divergence" title="Genetic divergence">diverged</a> about 1.2 million years more recently than the other chromosomes.</dd></dl>\n<p>The research suggests:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>There were in fact two splits between the human and chimp lineages, with the first being followed by interbreeding between the two populations and then a second split. The suggestion of a hybridization has startled <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">paleoanthropologists</a>, who nonetheless are treating the new genetic data seriously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_quest_for_the_earliest_hominin">The quest for the earliest hominin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The quest for the earliest hominin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the 1990s, several teams of paleoanthropologists were working throughout Africa looking for evidence of the earliest divergence of the hominin lineage from the great apes. In 1994, Meave Leakey discovered <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_anamensis" title="Australopithecus anamensis">Australopithecus anamensis</a></i>. The find was overshadowed by Tim D. White\'s 1995 discovery of <i>Ardipithecus ramidus</i>, which pushed back the fossil record to <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=4.2">4.2</a>&#160;million years ago</span>.\n</p><p>In 2000, <a href="/wiki/Martin_Pickford" title="Martin Pickford">Martin Pickford</a> and Brigitte Senut discovered, in the <a href="/wiki/Tugen_Hills" title="Tugen Hills">Tugen Hills</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, a 6-million-year-old bipedal hominin which they named <i><a href="/wiki/Orrorin" title="Orrorin">Orrorin tugenensis</a></i>. And in 2001, a team led by <a href="/wiki/Michel_Brunet_(paleontologist)" title="Michel Brunet (paleontologist)">Michel Brunet</a> discovered the skull of <i><a href="/wiki/Sahelanthropus" title="Sahelanthropus">Sahelanthropus tchadensis</a></i> which was dated as <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=7.2">7.2</a> million years ago</span>, and which Brunet argued was a bipedal, and therefore a hominid&#8212;that is, a hominin (<i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><abbr title="confer">cf</abbr></i> Hominidae; <a href="/wiki/Hominidae" title="Hominidae">terms "hominids" and hominins</a>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_dispersal">Human dispersal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Human dispersal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:628px;max-width:628px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:362px;max-width:362px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_map_of_prehistoric_human_migrations.jpg"><img alt="Map with arrows emanating from Africa, across Eurasia, to Australia and the Americas." data-file-height="635" data-file-width="889" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/World_map_of_prehistoric_human_migrations.jpg/360px-World_map_of_prehistoric_human_migrations.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/World_map_of_prehistoric_human_migrations.jpg/540px-World_map_of_prehistoric_human_migrations.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/World_map_of_prehistoric_human_migrations.jpg/720px-World_map_of_prehistoric_human_migrations.jpg 2x" width="360"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A global mapping model of human migration, based from divergence of the <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> (which indicates the <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(genetic)" title="Lineage (genetic)">matrilineage</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Timescale (<a href="/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers" title="Year">ka</a>) indicated by colours.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:262px;max-width:262px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_trellis.jpg"><img alt="Trellis of intermingling populations for the last two million years." data-file-height="594" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/66/Homo_trellis.jpg/260px-Homo_trellis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/66/Homo_trellis.jpg/390px-Homo_trellis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/66/Homo_trellis.jpg/520px-Homo_trellis.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A "trellis" (as <a href="/wiki/Milford_H._Wolpoff" title="Milford H. Wolpoff">Milford H. Wolpoff</a> called it) that emphasizes back-and-forth gene flow among geographic regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Different models for the beginning of the present human species.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Early_human_migrations" title="Early human migrations">Early human migrations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Recent African origin of modern humans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multiregional_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Multiregional origin of modern humans">Multiregional origin of modern humans</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_hominids_in_Southeast_Asia" title="Early hominids in Southeast Asia">Early hominids in Southeast Asia</a></div>\n<p>Anthropologists in the 1980s were divided regarding some details of reproductive barriers and migratory dispersals of the genus <i>Homo</i>. Subsequently, genetics has been used to investigate and resolve these issues. According to the <a href="/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory" title="Sahara pump theory">Sahara pump theory</a> evidence suggests that genus <i>Homo</i> have migrated out of Africa at least three and possibly four times (e.g. <i>Homo erectus</i>, <i>Homo heidelbergensis</i> and two or three times for <i>Homo sapiens</i>). Recent evidence suggests these dispersals are closely related to fluctuating periods of climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recent evidence suggests that humans may have left Africa half a million years earlier than previously thought.  A joint Franco-Indian team has found human artifacts in the Siwalk Hills north of New Delhi dating back at least 2.6 million years.  This is earlier than the previous earliest finding of genus <i>Homo</i> at <a href="/wiki/Dmanisi" title="Dmanisi">Dmanisi</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, dating to 1.85 million years.  Although controversial, tools found at a Chinese cave strengthen the case that  humans used tools as far back as 2.48 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>  This suggests that the Asian "Chopper" tool tradition, found in Java and northern China may have left Africa before the appearance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acheulian" title="Acheulian">Acheulian</a> hand axe.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dispersal_of_modern_Homo_sapiens">Dispersal of modern <i>Homo sapiens</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dispersal of modern Homo sapiens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Up until the genetic evidence became available there were two dominant models for the dispersal of modern humans.  The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiregional_Evolution" title="Multiregional Evolution">multiregional hypothesis</a> proposed that the genus <i>Homo</i> contained only a single interconnected population as it does today (not separate species), and that its evolution took place worldwide continuously over the last couple of million years. This model was proposed in 1988 by <a href="/wiki/Milford_H._Wolpoff" title="Milford H. Wolpoff">Milford H. Wolpoff</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiregional_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-multiregional-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wolpoff1988_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wolpoff1988-78">[78]</a></sup> In contrast the "out of Africa" model proposed that modern <i>H. sapiens</i> <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciated</a> in Africa recently (that is, approximately 200,000 years ago) and the subsequent migration through <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> resulted in nearly complete replacement of other <i>Homo</i> species. This model has been developed by <a href="/wiki/Chris_Stringer" title="Chris Stringer">Chris B. Stringer</a> and Peter Andrews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sequencing mtDNA and Y-DNA sampled from a wide range of indigenous populations revealed ancestral information relating to both male and female genetic heritage, and strengthened the Out of Africa theory and weakened the views of Multiregional Evolutionism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Aligned in genetic tree differences were interpreted as supportive of a recent single origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Analyses have shown a greater diversity of DNA patterns throughout Africa, consistent with the idea that Africa is the ancestral home of <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_Eve" title="Mitochondrial Eve">mitochondrial Eve</a> and <a href="/wiki/Y-chromosomal_Adam" title="Y-chromosomal Adam">Y-chromosomal Adam</a>, and that modern human dispersal out of Africa has only occurred over the last 55,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>"Out of Africa" has thus gained much support from research using female mitochondrial DNA and the male <a href="/wiki/Y_chromosome" title="Y chromosome">Y chromosome</a>. After analysing genealogy trees constructed using 133 types of mtDNA, researchers concluded that all were descended from a female African progenitor, dubbed <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_Eve" title="Mitochondrial Eve">Mitochondrial Eve</a>. "Out of Africa" is also supported by the fact that mitochondrial genetic diversity is highest among African populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cann_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cann-84">[84]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A broad study of African genetic diversity, headed by <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Tishkoff" title="Sarah Tishkoff">Sarah Tishkoff</a>, found the <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San people</a> had the greatest genetic diversity among the 113 distinct populations sampled, making them one of 14 "<a href="/wiki/Human_genetic_clustering" title="Human genetic clustering">ancestral population clusters</a>". The research also located a possible origin of modern human migration in south-western Africa, near the coastal border of <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The fossil evidence was insufficient for archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Leakey" title="Richard Leakey">Richard Leakey</a> to resolve the debate about exactly where in Africa modern humans first appeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Studies of <a href="/wiki/Haplogroup" title="Haplogroup">haplogroups</a> in <a href="/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup">Y-chromosomal DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">mitochondrial DNA</a> have largely supported a recent African origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> All the evidence from autosomal DNA also predominantly supports a Recent African origin. However, evidence for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">archaic admixture in modern humans</a>, both in Africa and later, throughout Eurasia has recently been suggested by a number of studies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wall,_J._D._2009_1823_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wall,_J._D._2009_1823-88">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recent sequencing of <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenetal_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-greenetal-89">[89]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21179161_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21179161-90">[90]</a></sup> genomes shows that some admixture with these populations has occurred. Modern humans outside Africa have 2&#8211;4% Neanderthal <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">alleles</a> in their genome, and some <a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesians</a> have an additional 4&#8211;6% of Denisovan alleles. These new results do not contradict the "out of Africa" model, except in its strictest interpretation, although they make the situation more complex. After recovery from a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_bottleneck" title="Genetic bottleneck">genetic bottleneck</a> that some researchers speculate might be linked to the <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba supervolcano catastrophe</a>, a fairly small group left Africa and interbred with Neanderthals, probably in the Middle East, on the Eurasian steppe or even in North Africa before their departure. Their still predominantly African descendants spread to populate the world. A fraction in turn interbred with Denisovans, probably in south-east Asia, before populating Melanesia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reich_2011_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reich_2011-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Human_leukocyte_antigen" title="Human leukocyte antigen">HLA</a> haplotypes of Neanderthal and Denisova origin have been identified in modern Eurasian and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceanian</a> populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1126/science.1209202_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-10.1126/science.1209202-92">[92]</a></sup>  The Denisovan <a href="/wiki/EPAS1" title="EPAS1">EPAS1</a> gene has also been found in Tibetan populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Studies of the human genome using machine learning have identified additional genetic contributions in Eurasians from an "unknown" ancestral population potentially related to the Neanderthal-Denisovan lineage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are still differing theories on whether there was a single exodus from Africa or several. A multiple dispersal model involves the Southern Dispersal theory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lahr_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-lahr-95">[95]</a></sup> which has gained support in recent years from genetic, linguistic and archaeological evidence. In this theory, there was a coastal dispersal of modern humans from the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> crossing the Bab el Mandib to Yemen at a lower sea level around 70,000 years ago. This group helped to populate Southeast Asia and Oceania, explaining the discovery of early human sites in these areas much earlier than those in the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lahr_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-lahr-95">[95]</a></sup>  This group seems to have been dependent upon marine resources for their survival.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Stephen_Oppenheimer" title="Stephen Oppenheimer">Stephen Oppenheimer</a> has proposed a second wave of humans may have later dispersed through the Persian Gulf oases, and the Zagros mountains into the Middle East.  Alternatively it may have come across the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a> into Asia, from shortly after 50,000 yrs BP, resulting in the bulk of the human populations of Eurasia. It has been suggested that this second group possibly possessed a more sophisticated "big game hunting" tool technology and was less dependent on coastal food sources than the original group. Much of the evidence for the first group\'s expansion would have been destroyed by the rising sea levels at the end of each <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glacial_maximum" title="Glacial maximum">glacial maximum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lahr_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-lahr-95">[95]</a></sup> The multiple dispersal model is contradicted by studies indicating that the populations of Eurasia and the populations of Southeast Asia and Oceania are all descended from the same mitochondrial DNA L3 <a href="/wiki/Lineage_(genetic)" title="Lineage (genetic)">lineages</a>, which support a single migration out of Africa that gave rise to all non-African populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Stephen Oppenheimer, on the basis of the early date of Badoshan Iranian Aurignacian, suggests that this second dispersal, may have occurred with a pluvial period about 50,000 years before the present, with modern human big-game hunting cultures spreading up the Zagros Mountains, carrying modern human genomes from Oman, throughout the Persian Gulf, northward into Armenia and Anatolia, with a variant travelling south into Israel and to Cyrenicia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oppenheimer_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimer-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence">Evidence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Evidence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The evidence on which scientific accounts of human evolution are based comes from many fields of <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a>. The main source of knowledge about the evolutionary process has traditionally been the fossil record, but since the development of genetics beginning in the 1970s, DNA analysis has come to occupy a place of comparable importance. The studies of ontogeny, <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetics" title="Phylogenetics">phylogeny</a> and especially <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_developmental_biology" title="Evolutionary developmental biology">evolutionary developmental biology</a> of both vertebrates and invertebrates offer considerable insight into the evolution of all life, including how humans evolved. The specific study of the origin and life of humans is <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a>, particularly paleoanthropology which focuses on the study of human <a href="/wiki/Prehistory" title="Prehistory">prehistory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence_from_molecular_biology">Evidence from molecular biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Evidence from molecular biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hominidae_chart.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="135" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Hominidae_chart.svg/330px-Hominidae_chart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Hominidae_chart.svg/495px-Hominidae_chart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Hominidae_chart.svg/660px-Hominidae_chart.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hominidae_chart.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Family tree showing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> hominoids: <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> (genus <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" title="Common chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bonobo" title="Bonobo">bonobos</a> (genus <i><a href="/wiki/Pan_(genus)" title="Pan (genus)">Pan</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Gorilla" title="Gorilla">gorillas</a> (genus <i>Gorilla</i>), orangutans (genus <i><a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">Pongo</a></i>), and gibbons (four genera of the family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hylobatidae" title="Hylobatidae">Hylobatidae</a>: <i><a href="/wiki/Hylobates" title="Hylobates">Hylobates</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoolock" title="Hoolock">Hoolock</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nomascus" title="Nomascus">Nomascus</a></i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symphalangus" title="Symphalangus">Symphalangus</a></i>). All except gibbons are hominids.</div></div></div>\n<p>The closest living relatives of humans are bonobos and chimpanzees (both genus <i>Pan</i>) and gorillas (genus <i>Gorilla</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-99">[99]</a></sup> With the sequencing of both the human and chimpanzee genome, as of  2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> estimates of the similarity between their DNA sequences range between 95% and 99%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sayers_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-sayers-101">[101]</a></sup> By using the technique called the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_clock" title="Molecular clock">molecular clock</a> which estimates the time required for the number of divergent mutations to accumulate between two lineages, the approximate date for the split between lineages can be calculated.\n</p><p>The gibbons (family Hylobatidae) and then orangutans (genus <i>Pongo</i>) were the first groups to split from the line leading to the hominins, including humans&#8212;followed by gorillas, and, ultimately, by the chimpanzees (genus <i>Pan</i>). The splitting date between hominin and chimpanzee lineages is placed by some between <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=4&#8211;8">4&#160;to&#160;8</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, that is, during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Miocene" title="Late Miocene">Late Miocene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins_R_2004_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawkins_R_2004-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ruvolo1997_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ruvolo1997-104">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">Speciation</a>, however, appears to have been unusually drawn-out. Initial divergence occurred sometime between <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=7&#8211;13">7&#160;to&#160;13</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, but ongoing hybridization blurred the separation and delayed complete separation during several millions of years.
