Patterson (2006) dated the final divergence at <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=5&#8211;6">5&#160;to&#160;6</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Genetic evidence has also been employed to resolve the question of whether there was any <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">gene flow between early modern humans and Neanderthals</a>, and to enhance our understanding of the early human migration patterns and splitting dates. By comparing the parts of the genome that are not under natural selection and which therefore accumulate mutations at a fairly steady rate, it is possible to reconstruct a genetic tree incorporating the entire human species since the last shared ancestor.\n</p><p>Each time a certain mutation (<a href="/wiki/Single-nucleotide_polymorphism" title="Single-nucleotide polymorphism">single-nucleotide polymorphism</a>) appears in an individual and is passed on to his or her descendants a haplogroup is formed including all of the descendants of the individual who will also carry that mutation. By comparing mitochondrial DNA which is inherited only from the mother, geneticists have concluded that the last female common ancestor whose genetic marker is found in all modern humans, the so-called mitochondrial Eve, must have lived around 200,000 years ago.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Human_evolutionary_genetics" title="Human evolutionary genetics">Human evolutionary genetics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_genetic_variation" title="Human genetic variation">Human genetic variation</a></div>\n<p>Human evolutionary genetics studies how one <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> differs from the other, the evolutionary past that gave rise to it, and its current effects. Differences between genomes have <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropological</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical" title="Medical">medical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forensic_science" title="Forensic science">forensic</a> implications and applications. Genetic data can provide important insight into human evolution.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evidence_from_the_fossil_record">Evidence from the fossil record</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Evidence from the fossil record">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1321" data-file-width="1006" decoding="async" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg/170px-Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg/255px-Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg/340px-Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replica of fossil skull of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_habilis" title="Homo habilis">Homo habilis</a></i>. Fossil number KNM ER 1813, found at <a href="/wiki/Koobi_Fora" title="Koobi Fora">Koobi Fora</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_ergaster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Homo_ergaster.jpg/170px-Homo_ergaster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Homo_ergaster.jpg/255px-Homo_ergaster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Homo_ergaster.jpg/340px-Homo_ergaster.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_ergaster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Replica of fossil skull of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">Homo ergaster</a></i> (African <i>Homo erectus</i>). Fossil number Khm-Heu 3733 discovered in 1975 in Kenya.</div></div></div>\n<p>There is little fossil evidence for the divergence of the gorilla, chimpanzee and hominin lineages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The earliest fossils that have been proposed as members of the hominin lineage are <i>Sahelanthropus tchadensis</i> dating from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=7">7</a>&#160;million years ago</span>, <i>Orrorin tugenensis</i> dating from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=5.7">5.7</a>&#160;million years ago</span>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Ardipithecus#Ardipithecus_kadabba" title="Ardipithecus">Ardipithecus kadabba</a></i> dating to <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=5.6">5.6</a>&#160;million years ago</span>. Each of these have been argued to be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bipedal" title="Bipedal">bipedal</a> ancestor of later hominins but, in each case, the claims have been contested. It is also possible that one or more of these species are ancestors of another branch of African apes, or that they represent a shared ancestor between hominins and other apes.\n</p><p>The question then of the relationship between these early fossil species and the hominin lineage is still to be resolved. From these early species, the australopithecines arose around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=4">4</a>&#160;million years ago</span> and diverged into <a href="/wiki/Paranthropus" title="Paranthropus">robust</a> (also called <i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus" title="Paranthropus">Paranthropus</a></i>) and <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">gracile</a> branches, one of which (possibly <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_garhi" title="Australopithecus garhi">A. garhi</a></i>) probably went on to become ancestors of the genus <i>Homo</i>. The australopithecine species that is best represented in the fossil record is <i>Australopithecus afarensis</i> with more than one hundred fossil individuals represented, found from Northern Ethiopia (such as the famous "Lucy"), to Kenya, and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>. Fossils of robust australopithecines such as <i>Au. robustus</i> (or alternatively <i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus_robustus" title="Paranthropus robustus">Paranthropus robustus</a></i>) and <i>Au./P. boisei</i> are particularly abundant in South Africa at sites such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kromdraai" title="Kromdraai">Kromdraai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swartkrans" title="Swartkrans">Swartkrans</a>, and around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Turkana" title="Lake Turkana">Lake Turkana</a> in Kenya.\n</p><p>The earliest member of the genus <i>Homo</i> is <i>Homo habilis</i> which evolved around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=2.8">2.8</a>&#160;million years ago</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-107">[107]</a></sup> <i>Homo habilis</i> is the first species for which we have positive evidence of the use of stone tools. They developed the <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a> lithic technology, named after the Olduvai Gorge in which the first specimens were found. Some scientists consider <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_rudolfensis" title="Homo rudolfensis">Homo rudolfensis</a></i>, a larger bodied group of fossils with similar morphology to the original <i>H. habilis</i> fossils, to be a separate species while others consider them to be part of <i>H. habilis</i>&#8212;simply representing intraspecies variation, or perhaps even <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a>. The brains of these early hominins were about the same size as that of a chimpanzee, and their main adaptation was bipedalism as an adaptation to terrestrial living.\n</p><p>During the next million years, a process of encephalization began and, by the arrival (about <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1.9">1.9</a>&#160;million years ago</span>) of <i>Homo erectus</i> in the fossil record, cranial capacity had doubled. <i>Homo erectus</i> were the first of the hominins to emigrate from Africa, and, from <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1.8&#8211;1.3">1.8&#160;to&#160;1.3</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>, this species spread through Africa, Asia, and Europe. One population of <i>H. erectus</i>, also sometimes classified as a separate species <i>Homo ergaster</i>, remained in Africa and evolved into <i>Homo sapiens</i>. It is believed that these species, <i>H. erectus</i> and <i>H. ergaster</i>, were the first to use fire and complex tools.\n</p><p>The earliest transitional fossils between <i>H. ergaster/erectus</i> and archaic <i>H. sapiens</i> are from Africa, such as <i>Homo rhodesiensis</i>, but seemingly transitional forms were also found at <a href="/wiki/Dmanisi" title="Dmanisi">Dmanisi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>. These descendants of African <i>H. erectus</i> spread through Eurasia from ca. 500,000 years ago evolving into <i>H. antecessor</i>, <i>H. heidelbergensis</i> and <i>H. neanderthalensis</i>. The earliest fossils of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a> are from the Middle Paleolithic, about 200,000 years ago such as the <a href="/wiki/Omo_remains" title="Omo remains">Omo remains</a> of Ethiopia; later fossils from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skhul_and_Qafzeh_hominids" title="Skhul and Qafzeh hominids">Es Skhul</a> cave in <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and Southern Europe begin around 90,000 years ago (<span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=0.09">0.09</a>&#160;million years ago</span>).\n</p><p>As modern humans spread out from Africa, they encountered other hominins such as <i>Homo neanderthalensis</i> and the so-called Denisovans, who may have evolved from populations of <i>Homo erectus</i> that had left Africa around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=2">2</a>&#160;million years ago</span>. The nature of interaction between early humans and these sister species has been a long-standing source of controversy, the question being whether humans replaced these earlier species or whether they were in fact similar enough to interbreed, in which case these earlier populations may have contributed genetic material to modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-01302012_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-01302012-109">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This migration out of Africa is estimated to have begun about 70,000 years <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a> and modern humans subsequently spread globally, replacing earlier hominins either through competition or hybridization. They inhabited Eurasia and Oceania by 40,000 years BP, and the Americas by at least 14,500 years BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inter-species_breeding">Inter-species breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Inter-species breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Interbreeding_between_archaic_and_modern_humans" title="Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans">Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans</a></div>\n<p>The hypothesis of interbreeding, also known as hybridization, admixture or hybrid-origin theory, has been discussed ever since the discovery of Neanderthal remains in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The linear view of human evolution began to be abandoned in the 1970s as different species of humans were discovered that made the linear concept increasingly unlikely. In the 21st century with the advent of molecular biology techniques and computerization, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whole-genome_sequencing" title="Whole-genome sequencing">whole-genome sequencing</a> of Neanderthal and human <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> were performed, confirming recent admixture between different human species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greenetal_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-greenetal-89">[89]</a></sup> In 2010, evidence based on molecular biology was published, revealing unambiguous examples of interbreeding between archaic and modern humans during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>.  It has been demonstrated that interbreeding happened in several independent events that included Neanderthals and Denisovans, as well as several unidentified hominins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Today, approximately 2% of DNA from most Europeans and Asians is Neanderthal, with traces of Denisovan heritage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20180822_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20180822-113">[113]</a></sup> Also, 4&#8211;6% of modern <a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesian</a> genetics are Denisovan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NG-20180822_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-NG-20180822-113">[113]</a></sup>  Comparisons of the human genome to the genomes of Neandertals, Denisovans and apes can help identify features that set modern humans apart from other hominin species. In a 2016 <a href="/wiki/Comparative_genomics" title="Comparative genomics">comparative genomics</a> study, a Harvard Medical School/UCLA research team made a world map on the distribution and made some predictions about where Denisovan and Neanderthal genes may be impacting modern human biology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For example, comparative studies in the mid-2010s found several <a href="/wiki/Phenotypic_trait" title="Phenotypic trait">traits</a> related to neurological, immunological,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> developmental, and metabolic phenotypes, that were developed by archaic humans to European and Asian environments and inherited to modern humans through admixture with local hominins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the narratives of human evolution are often contentious, several discoveries since 2010 show that human evolution should not be seen as a simple linear or branched progression, but a mix of related species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21179161_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21179161-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Denisovans_&amp;_Neandertals_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Denisovans_&amp;_Neandertals-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Human_Hybrids_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Hybrids-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mosaic_humans,_the_hybrid_species_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mosaic_humans,_the_hybrid_species-5">[5]</a></sup> In fact, genomic research has shown that hybridization between substantially diverged lineages is the rule, not the exception, in human evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ackermann_2015_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ackermann_2015-2">[2]</a></sup> Furthermore, it is argued that hybridization was an essential creative force in the emergence of modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ackermann_2015_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ackermann_2015-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Before_Homo">Before <i>Homo</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Before Homo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For evolutionary history before primates, see <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_mammals" title="Evolution of mammals">Evolution of mammals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_history_of_life" title="Evolutionary history of life">Evolutionary history of life</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_human_evolution" title="Timeline of human evolution">Timeline of human evolution</a>.</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_evolution_of_primates">Early evolution of primates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Early evolution of primates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_primates" title="Evolution of primates">Evolution of primates</a></div>\n<p>Evolutionary history of the primates can be traced back 65 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> One of the oldest known primate-like mammal species, the <i><a href="/wiki/Plesiadapis" title="Plesiadapis">Plesiadapis</a></i>, came from North America;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> another, <i><a href="/wiki/Archicebus" title="Archicebus">Archicebus</a></i>, came from China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20130605_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130605-126">[126]</a></sup> Other similar basal primates were widespread in Eurasia and Africa during the tropical conditions of the Paleocene and <a href="/wiki/Eocene" title="Eocene">Eocene</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1042" data-file-width="1440" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg/220px-Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg/330px-Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg/440px-Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Notharctus_tenebrosus" title="Notharctus tenebrosus">Notharctus tenebrosus</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="American Museum of Natural History">American Museum of Natural History</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, US.</div></div></div>\n<p>David R. Begun <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kordos-p17_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kordos-p17-127">[127]</a></sup> concluded that early primates flourished in Eurasia and that a lineage leading to the African apes and humans, including to <i><a href="/wiki/Dryopithecus" title="Dryopithecus">Dryopithecus</a></i>, migrated south from Europe or Western Asia into Africa. The surviving tropical population of primates&#8212;which is seen most completely in the Upper Eocene and lowermost <a href="/wiki/Oligocene" title="Oligocene">Oligocene</a> fossil beds of the <a href="/wiki/Faiyum" title="Faiyum">Faiyum</a> depression southwest of <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>&#8212;gave rise to all extant primate species, including the <a href="/wiki/Lemur" title="Lemur">lemurs</a> of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Loris" title="Loris">lorises</a> of Southeast Asia, <a href="/wiki/Galago" title="Galago">galagos</a> or "bush babies" of Africa, and to the <a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">anthropoids</a>, which are the <a href="/wiki/New_World_monkey" title="New World monkey">Platyrrhines</a> or New World monkeys, the <a href="/wiki/Catarrhini" title="Catarrhini">Catarrhines</a> or Old World monkeys, and the great apes, including humans and other hominids.\n</p><p>The earliest known <a href="/wiki/Catarrhini" title="Catarrhini">catarrhine</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/Kamoyapithecus" title="Kamoyapithecus">Kamoyapithecus</a></i> from uppermost Oligocene at Eragaleit in the northern <a href="/wiki/Great_Rift_Valley,_Kenya" title="Great Rift Valley, Kenya">Great Rift Valley</a> in Kenya, dated to 24 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Its ancestry is thought to be species related to <i><a href="/wiki/Aegyptopithecus" title="Aegyptopithecus">Aegyptopithecus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Propliopithecus" title="Propliopithecus">Propliopithecus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Parapithecus" title="Parapithecus">Parapithecus</a></i> from the Faiyum, at around 35 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> In 2010, <i><a href="/wiki/Saadanius" title="Saadanius">Saadanius</a></i> was described as a close relative of the last common ancestor of the <a href="/wiki/Crown_group" title="Crown group">crown</a> catarrhines, and tentatively dated to 29&#8211;28 million years ago, helping to fill an 11-million-year gap in the fossil record.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2010Zalmout_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-2010Zalmout-130">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_(University_of_Zurich).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_%28University_of_Zurich%29.JPG/220px-Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_%28University_of_Zurich%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_%28University_of_Zurich%29.JPG/330px-Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_%28University_of_Zurich%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_%28University_of_Zurich%29.JPG/440px-Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_%28University_of_Zurich%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_(University_of_Zurich).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstructed tailless <i><a href="/wiki/Proconsul_(mammal)" title="Proconsul (mammal)">Proconsul</a></i> skeleton</div></div></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Early_Miocene" title="Early Miocene">Early Miocene</a>, about 22 million years ago, the many kinds of <a href="/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion" title="Arboreal locomotion">arboreally</a> adapted primitive catarrhines from East Africa suggest a long history of prior diversification. Fossils at 20 million years ago include fragments attributed to <i><a href="/wiki/Victoriapithecus" title="Victoriapithecus">Victoriapithecus</a></i>, the earliest Old World monkey. Among the genera thought to be in the ape lineage leading up to 13 million years ago are <i><a href="/wiki/Proconsul_(mammal)" title="Proconsul (mammal)">Proconsul</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rangwapithecus" title="Rangwapithecus">Rangwapithecus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Dendropithecus" title="Dendropithecus">Dendropithecus</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Limnopithecus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Limnopithecus (page does not exist)">Limnopithecus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nacholapithecus" title="Nacholapithecus">Nacholapithecus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Equatorius" title="Equatorius">Equatorius</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Nyanzapithecus_pickfordi" title="Nyanzapithecus pickfordi">Nyanzapithecus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Afropithecus" title="Afropithecus">Afropithecus</a></i>, <i>Heliopithecus</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Kenyapithecus" title="Kenyapithecus">Kenyapithecus</a></i>, all from East Africa.\n</p><p>The presence of other generalized non-cercopithecids of <a href="/wiki/Middle_Miocene" title="Middle Miocene">Middle Miocene</a> from sites far distant&#8212;<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Otavipithecus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Otavipithecus (page does not exist)">Otavipithecus</a></i> from cave deposits in Namibia, and <i><a href="/wiki/Pierolapithecus" title="Pierolapithecus">Pierolapithecus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Dryopithecus" title="Dryopithecus">Dryopithecus</a></i> from France, Spain and Austria&#8212;is evidence of a wide diversity of forms across Africa and the Mediterranean basin during the relatively warm and equable climatic regimes of the Early and Middle Miocene. The youngest of the <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> hominoids, <i><a href="/wiki/Oreopithecus" title="Oreopithecus">Oreopithecus</a></i>, is from coal beds in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> that have been dated to 9 million years ago.\n</p><p>Molecular evidence indicates that the lineage of gibbons (family Hylobatidae) diverged from the line of great apes some 18&#8211;12 million years ago, and that of orangutans (subfamily Ponginae) diverged from the other great apes at about 12 million years; there are no fossils that clearly document the ancestry of gibbons, which may have originated in a so-far-unknown South East Asian hominoid population, but fossil proto-orangutans may be represented by <i><a href="/wiki/Sivapithecus" title="Sivapithecus">Sivapithecus</a></i> from India and <i><a href="/wiki/Griphopithecus" title="Griphopithecus">Griphopithecus</a></i> from Turkey, dated to around 10 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Srivastava2009_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Srivastava2009-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divergence_of_the_human_clade_from_other_great_apes">Divergence of the human clade from other great apes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Divergence of the human clade from other great apes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucy-reconstruction.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Lucy-reconstruction.jpg/170px-Lucy-reconstruction.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Lucy-reconstruction.jpg/255px-Lucy-reconstruction.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Lucy-reconstruction.jpg/340px-Lucy-reconstruction.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucy-reconstruction.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A reconstruction of "<a href="/wiki/Lucy_(Australopithecus)" title="Lucy (Australopithecus)">Lucy</a>", a female <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Australopithecus afarensis</a></i> on exhibit in the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="National Museum of Natural History">National Museum of Natural History</a>, <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, US.</div></div></div>\n<p>Species close to the last common ancestor of gorillas, chimpanzees and humans may be represented by <i><a href="/wiki/Nakalipithecus" title="Nakalipithecus">Nakalipithecus</a></i> fossils found in Kenya and <i><a href="/wiki/Ouranopithecus" title="Ouranopithecus">Ouranopithecus</a></i> found in Greece. Molecular evidence suggests that between 8 and 4 million years ago, first the gorillas, and then the chimpanzees (genus <i>Pan</i>) split off from the line leading to the humans. Human DNA is approximately 98.4% identical to that of chimpanzees when comparing single nucleotide polymorphisms (see <a href="/wiki/Human_evolutionary_genetics" title="Human evolutionary genetics">human evolutionary genetics</a>). The fossil record, however, of gorillas and chimpanzees is limited; both poor preservation&#8212;rain forest soils tend to be acidic and dissolve bone&#8212;and <a href="/wiki/Sampling_bias" title="Sampling bias">sampling bias</a> probably contribute to this problem.\n</p><p>Other hominins probably adapted to the drier environments outside the equatorial belt; and there they encountered antelope, hyenas, dogs, pigs, elephants, horses, and others. The equatorial belt contracted after about 8 million years ago, and there is very little fossil evidence for the split&#8212;thought to have occurred around that time&#8212;of the hominin lineage from the lineages of gorillas and chimpanzees. The earliest fossils argued by some to belong to the human lineage are <i>Sahelanthropus tchadensis</i> (7 Ma) and <i>Orrorin tugenensis</i> (6 Ma), followed by <i>Ardipithecus</i> (5.5&#8211;4.4 Ma), with species <i>Ar. kadabba</i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ar._ramidus" title="Ar. ramidus">Ar. ramidus</a></i>.\n</p><p>It has been argued in a study of the life history of <i>Ar. ramidus</i> that the species provides evidence for a suite of anatomical and behavioral adaptations in very early hominins unlike any species of extant great ape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramidus_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-aramidus-131">[131]</a></sup> This study demonstrated affinities between the skull morphology of <i>Ar. ramidus</i> and that of infant and juvenile chimpanzees, suggesting the species evolved a juvenalised or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paedomorphic" title="Paedomorphic">paedomorphic</a> craniofacial morphology via <a href="/wiki/Heterochrony" title="Heterochrony">heterochronic</a> dissociation of growth trajectories. It was also argued that the species provides support for the notion that very early hominins, akin to bonobos (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pan_paniscus" title="Pan paniscus">Pan paniscus</a></i>) the less aggressive species of the genus <i>Pan</i>, may have evolved via the process of <a href="/wiki/Self-domestication" title="Self-domestication">self-domestication</a>. Consequently, arguing against the so-called "chimpanzee referential model"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sayers_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-sayers-101">[101]</a></sup> the authors suggest it is no longer tenable to use <a href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" title="Common chimpanzee">common chimpanzee</a> (<i>Pan troglodytes</i>) social and mating behaviors in models of early hominin social evolution. When commenting on the absence of aggressive canine morphology in <i>Ar. ramidus</i> and the implications this has for the evolution of hominin social psychology, they wrote:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Of course <i>Ar. ramidus</i>  differs significantly from bonobos, bonobos having retained a functional canine honing complex. However, the fact that <i>Ar. ramidus</i> shares with bonobos reduced sexual dimorphism, and a more paedomorphic form relative to chimpanzees, suggests that the developmental and social adaptations evident in bonobos may be of assistance in future reconstructions of early hominin social and sexual psychology. In fact the trend towards increased maternal care, female mate selection and self-domestication may have been stronger and more refined in  <i>Ar. ramidus</i> than what we see in bonobos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aramidus_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-aramidus-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:<span>128</span></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The authors argue that many of the basic human adaptations evolved in the ancient forest and woodland ecosystems of late <a href="/wiki/Miocene" title="Miocene">Miocene</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a> Africa. Consequently, they argue that humans may not represent evolution from a chimpanzee-like ancestor as has traditionally been supposed. This suggests many modern human adaptations represent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogenetically</a> deep traits and that the behavior and morphology of chimpanzees may have evolved subsequent to the split with the common ancestor they share with humans.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genus_Australopithecus">Genus <i>Australopithecus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Genus Australopithecus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a></div>\n<p>The genus <i>Australopithecus</i> evolved in eastern Africa around 4 million years ago before spreading throughout the continent and eventually becoming extinct 2 million years ago. During this time period various forms of australopiths existed, including <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_anamensis" title="Australopithecus anamensis">Australopithecus anamensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Au. afarensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_sediba" title="Australopithecus sediba">Au. sediba</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_africanus" title="Australopithecus africanus">Au. africanus</a></i>. There is still some debate among academics whether certain African hominid species of this time, such as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australopithecus_robustus" title="Australopithecus robustus">Au. robustus</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Australopithecus_boisei" title="Australopithecus boisei">Au. boisei</a></i>, constitute members of the same genus; if so, they would be considered to be <i>Au. robust australopiths</i> whilst the others would be considered <i>Au. gracile australopiths</i>. However, if these species do indeed constitute their own genus, then they may be given their own name, the <i>Paranthropus</i>.\n</p>\n<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus" title="Australopithecus">Australopithecus</a></i> (4&#8211;1.8 Ma), with species <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_anamensis" title="Australopithecus anamensis">Au. anamensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Au. afarensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_africanus" title="Australopithecus africanus">Au. africanus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_bahrelghazali" title="Australopithecus bahrelghazali">Au. bahrelghazali</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_garhi" title="Australopithecus garhi">Au. garhi</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_sediba" title="Australopithecus sediba">Au. sediba</a></i>;</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Kenyanthropus" title="Kenyanthropus">Kenyanthropus</a></i> (3&#8211;2.7 Ma), with species <i><a href="/wiki/Kenyanthropus" title="Kenyanthropus">K. platyops</a></i>;</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus" title="Paranthropus">Paranthropus</a></i> (3&#8211;1.2 Ma), with species <i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus_aethiopicus" title="Paranthropus aethiopicus">P. aethiopicus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus_boisei" title="Paranthropus boisei">P. boisei</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus_robustus" title="Paranthropus robustus">P. robustus</a></i></li></ul>\n<p>A new proposed species <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_deyiremeda" title="Australopithecus deyiremeda">Australopithecus deyiremeda</a></i> is claimed to have been discovered living at the same time period of <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Au. afarensis</a></i>.  There is debate if <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Au.</a> <a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_deyiremeda" title="Australopithecus deyiremeda">deyiremeda</a> is a new species or is <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Au. afarensis</a>.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> <i>Australopithecus prometheus</i>, otherwise known as <a href="/wiki/Little_Foot" title="Little Foot">Little Foot</a> has recently been dated at 3.67 million years old through a new dating technique, making the genus <i>Australopithecus</i> as old as <i>afarensis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>  Given the opposable big toe found on Little Foot, it seems that he was a good climber, and it is thought given the night predators of the region, he probably, like gorillas and chimpanzees, built a nesting platform at night, in the trees.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_genus_Homo">Evolution of genus <i>Homo</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Evolution of genus Homo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></div>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-move" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="100" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="17" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/50px-Split-arrows.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/75px-Split-arrows.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/100px-Split-arrows.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">It has been suggested that this section be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting" title="Wikipedia:Splitting">split</a> out  into another article titled <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i>. (<a href="/wiki/Talk:Human_evolution" title="Talk:Human evolution">Discuss</a>) <small><i>(April 2018)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The earliest documented representative of the genus <i>Homo</i> is <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_habilis" title="Homo habilis">Homo habilis</a></i>, which evolved around <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=2.8">2.8</a>&#160;million years ago</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-107">[107]</a></sup> and is arguably the earliest species for which there is positive evidence of the use of stone tools. The brains of these early hominins were about the same size as that of a <a href="/wiki/Common_chimpanzee" title="Common chimpanzee">chimpanzee</a>, although it has been suggested that this was the time in which the human <a href="/wiki/SRGAP2" title="SRGAP2">SRGAP2</a> <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">gene</a> doubled, producing a more rapid wiring of the frontal cortex. During the next million years a process of rapid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Encephalization" title="Encephalization">encephalization</a> occurred, and with the arrival of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">Homo ergaster</a></i> in the <a href="/wiki/Fossil#Fossil_record" title="Fossil">fossil record</a>, cranial capacity had doubled to 850&#160;cm<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> (Such an increase in human brain size is equivalent to each generation having 125,000 more <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> than their parents.) It is believed that <i>Homo erectus</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">Homo ergaster</a></i> were the first to use fire and complex tools, and were the first of the hominin line to leave Africa, spreading throughout Africa, Asia, and Europe between <span class="nourlexpansion plainlinks" style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/timescale/?Ma=1.3&#8211;1.8">1.3&#160;to&#160;1.8</a> <a href="/wiki/Myr" title="Myr">million years ago</a></span>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_lineage_2017update.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="675" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Homo_lineage_2017update.svg/310px-Homo_lineage_2017update.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Homo_lineage_2017update.svg/465px-Homo_lineage_2017update.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Homo_lineage_2017update.svg/620px-Homo_lineage_2017update.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_lineage_2017update.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A model of the evolution of the genus <i>Homo</i> over the last 2 million years (vertical axis). The rapid "<a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Out of Africa</a>" expansion of <i>H. sapiens</i> is indicated at the top of the diagram, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">admixture</a> indicated with  Neanderthals, Denisovans, and unspecified archaic African hominins. Late survival of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robust_australopithecines" title="Robust australopithecines">robust australopithecines</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus" title="Paranthropus">Paranthropus</a></i>) alongside <i>Homo</i> until 1.2 Mya is indicated in purple.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_sapiens_lineage.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="632" data-file-width="722" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Homo_sapiens_lineage.svg/310px-Homo_sapiens_lineage.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Homo_sapiens_lineage.svg/465px-Homo_sapiens_lineage.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Homo_sapiens_lineage.svg/620px-Homo_sapiens_lineage.svg.png 2x" width="310"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_sapiens_lineage.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A model of the phylogeny of <i>H. sapiens</i> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a>. The horizontal axis represents geographic location; the vertical axis represents time in <a href="/wiki/Year#Abbreviations_yr_and_ya" title="Year">thousands of years ago</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <i>Homo heidelbergensis</i>  is shown as diverging into Neanderthals, Denisovans and <i>H. sapiens</i>. With the expansion of <i>H. sapiens</i> after 200 kya, Neanderthals, Denisovans and unspecified archaic African hominins are shown as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">again subsumed</a> into the <i>H. sapiens</i> lineage. In addition, admixture events in modern African populations are indicated.</div></div></div>\n<p>According to the recent African origin of modern humans theory, modern humans evolved in Africa possibly from <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">Homo heidelbergensis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_rhodesiensis" title="Homo rhodesiensis">Homo rhodesiensis</a></i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_antecessor" title="Homo antecessor">Homo antecessor</a></i> and migrated out of the continent some 50,000 to 100,000 years ago, gradually replacing local populations of <i>Homo erectus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisova hominins</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_floresiensis" title="Homo floresiensis">Homo floresiensis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Homo neanderthalensis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encylopediahumanevolution_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-encylopediahumanevolution-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evolutionthe1st4billionyears_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-evolutionthe1st4billionyears-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">Archaic <i>Homo sapiens</i></a>, the forerunner of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a>, evolved in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> between 400,000 and 250,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> Recent <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> evidence suggests that several <a href="/wiki/Haplotype" title="Haplotype">haplotypes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> origin are present among all non-African populations, and Neanderthals and other hominins, such as <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovans</a>, may have contributed up to 6% of their <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genome</a> to present-day humans, suggestive of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">limited inter-breeding between these species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21179161_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21179161-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20439435_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20439435-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1126/science.1209202_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-10.1126/science.1209202-92">[92]</a></sup>  The transition to <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">behavioral modernity</a> with the development of symbolic culture, language, and specialized <a href="/wiki/Lithic_technology" title="Lithic technology">lithic technology</a> happened around 50,000 years ago according to some anthropologists<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> although others point to evidence that suggests that a gradual change in behavior took place over a longer time span.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mcbrearty_Brooks_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mcbrearty_Brooks-146">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Homo sapiens</i> is the only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> species of its genus, <i>Homo</i>. While some (extinct) <i>Homo</i> species might have been ancestors of <i>Homo sapiens</i>, many, perhaps most, were likely "cousins", having speciated away from the ancestral hominin line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bryson2004_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-bryson2004-148">[148]</a></sup> There is yet no consensus as to which of these groups should be considered a separate species and which should be a subspecies; this may be due to the dearth of fossils or to the slight differences used to classify species in the genus <i>Homo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bryson2004_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-bryson2004-148">[148]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory" title="Sahara pump theory">Sahara pump theory</a> (describing an occasionally passable <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Subpluvial" title="Neolithic Subpluvial">"wet" Sahara</a> desert) provides one possible explanation of the early variation in the genus <i>Homo</i>.\n</p><p>Based on archaeological and paleontological evidence, it has been possible to infer, to some extent, the ancient dietary practices<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150813_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150813-34">[34]</a></sup> of various <i>Homo</i> species and to study the role of diet in physical and behavioral evolution within <i>Homo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard_2007_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard_2007-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some anthropologists and archaeologists subscribe to the <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba catastrophe theory</a>, which posits that the <a href="/wiki/Supervolcano" title="Supervolcano">supereruption</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Toba" title="Lake Toba">Lake Toba</a> on Sumatran island in Indonesia some 70,000 years ago caused global consequences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> killing the majority of humans and creating a <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">population bottleneck</a> that affected the genetic inheritance of all humans today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The genetic and archaeological evidence for this remains in question however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="H._habilis_and_H._gautengensis"><i>H. habilis</i> and <i>H. gautengensis</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: H. habilis and H. gautengensis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>Homo habilis</i> lived from about 2.8<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-107">[107]</a></sup> to 1.4 Ma. The species evolved in South and East Africa in the <a href="/wiki/Piacenzian" title="Piacenzian">Late Pliocene</a> or <a href="/wiki/Early_Pleistocene" title="Early Pleistocene">Early Pleistocene</a>, 2.5&#8211;2 Ma, when it diverged from the australopithecines. <i>Homo habilis</i> had smaller molars and larger brains than the australopithecines, and made tools from stone and perhaps animal bones. One of the first known hominins was nicknamed \'handy man\' by discoverer <a href="/wiki/Louis_Leakey" title="Louis Leakey">Louis Leakey</a> due to its association with <a href="/wiki/Stone_tool" title="Stone tool">stone tools</a>. Some scientists have proposed moving this species out of <i>Homo</i> and into <i>Australopithecus</i> due to the morphology of its skeleton being more adapted to <a href="/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion" title="Arboreal locomotion">living on trees</a> rather than to <a href="/wiki/Bipedalism" title="Bipedalism">moving on two legs</a> like <i>Homo sapiens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In May 2010, a new species, <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_gautengensis" title="Homo gautengensis">Homo gautengensis</a></i>, was discovered in South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toothy_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-toothy-157">[157]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="H._rudolfensis_and_H._georgicus"><i>H. rudolfensis</i> and <i>H. georgicus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: H. rudolfensis and H. georgicus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>These are proposed species names for fossils from about 1.9&#8211;1.6 Ma, whose relation to <i>Homo habilis</i> is not yet clear.\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>Homo rudolfensis</i> refers to a single, incomplete skull from Kenya. Scientists have suggested that this was another <i>Homo habilis</i>, but this has not been confirmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_georgicus" title="Homo georgicus">Homo georgicus</a></i>, from <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, may be an intermediate form between <i>Homo habilis</i> and <i>Homo erectus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> or a sub-species of <i>Homo erectus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="H._ergaster_and_H._erectus"><i>H. ergaster</i> and <i>H. erectus</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: H. ergaster and H. erectus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The first fossils of <i>Homo erectus</i> were discovered by Dutch physician <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_Dubois" title="Eugene Dubois">Eugene Dubois</a> in 1891 on the <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian</a> island of Java. He originally named the material <i><a href="/wiki/Anthropopithecus" title="Anthropopithecus">Anthropopithecus erectus</a></i> (1892&#8211;1893, considered at this point as a chimpanzee-like fossil primate) and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pithecanthropus" title="Pithecanthropus">Pithecanthropus erectus</a></i> (1893&#8211;1894, changing his mind as of based on its morphology, which he considered to be intermediate between that of humans and apes).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Years later, in the <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">20th century</a>, the German <a href="/wiki/Physician" title="Physician">physician</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">paleoanthropologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Franz_Weidenreich" title="Franz Weidenreich">Franz Weidenreich</a> (1873&#8211;1948) compared in detail the characters of Dubois\' <a href="/wiki/Java_Man" title="Java Man">Java Man</a>, then named <i>Pithecanthropus erectus</i>, with the characters of the <a href="/wiki/Peking_Man" title="Peking Man">Peking Man</a>, then named <i>Sinanthropus pekinensis</i>. Weidenreich concluded in 1940 that because of their anatomical similarity with modern humans it was necessary to gather all these specimens of Java and China in a single species of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Homo" title="Homo">Homo</a></i>, the species <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_erectus" title="Homo erectus">Homo erectus</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <i>Homo erectus</i> lived from about 1.8 Ma to about 70,000 years ago&#8212;which would indicate that they were probably wiped out by the Toba catastrophe; however, nearby <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_floresiensis" title="Homo floresiensis">Homo floresiensis</a></i> survived it. The early phase of <i>Homo erectus</i>, from 1.8 to 1.25 Ma, is considered by some to be a separate species, <i>Homo ergaster</i>, or as <i>Homo erectus ergaster</i>, a subspecies of <i>Homo erectus</i>.\n</p><p>In Africa in the Early Pleistocene, 1.5&#8211;1 Ma, some populations of <i>Homo habilis</i> are thought to have evolved larger brains and to have made more elaborate stone tools; these differences and others are sufficient for anthropologists to classify them as a new species, <i>Homo erectus</i>&#8212;in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> The evolution of locking knees and the movement of the foramen magnum are thought to be likely drivers of the larger population changes. This species also may have used fire to cook meat. <a href="/wiki/Catching_Fire:_How_Cooking_Made_Us_Human" title="Catching Fire: How Cooking Made Us Human">Richard Wrangham</a> suggests that the fact that Homo seems to have been ground dwelling, with reduced intestinal length, smaller dentition, "and swelled our brains to their current, horrendously fuel-inefficient size",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> suggest that control of fire and releasing increased nutritional value through cooking was the key adaptation that separated Homo from tree-sleeping Australopithecines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans" title="Control of fire by early humans">Control of fire by early humans</a></div>\n<p>A famous example of <i>Homo erectus</i> is <a href="/wiki/Peking_Man" title="Peking Man">Peking Man</a>; others were found in Asia (notably in Indonesia), Africa, and Europe. Many paleoanthropologists now use the term <i>Homo ergaster</i> for the non-Asian forms of this group, and reserve <i>Homo erectus</i> only for those fossils that are found in Asia and meet certain skeletal and dental requirements which differ slightly from <i>H. ergaster</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="H._cepranensis_and_H._antecessor"><i>H. cepranensis</i> and <i>H. antecessor</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: H. cepranensis and H. antecessor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>These are proposed as species that may be intermediate between <i>H. erectus</i> and <i>H. heidelbergensis</i>.\n</p>\n<ul><li><i>H. antecessor</i> is known from fossils from Spain and <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> that are dated 1.2 Ma&#8211;500 <a href="/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers" title="Year">ka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></li>\n<li><i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_cepranensis" title="Homo cepranensis">H. cepranensis</a></i> refers to a single skull cap from Italy, estimated to be about 800,000 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="H._heidelbergensis"><i>H. heidelbergensis</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: H. heidelbergensis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">Homo heidelbergensis</a></div>\n<p><i>H. heidelbergensis</i> ("Heidelberg Man") lived from about 800,000 to about 300,000 years ago. Also proposed as <i>Homo sapiens heidelbergensis</i> or <i>Homo sapiens paleohungaricus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="H._rhodesiensis.2C_and_the_Gawis_cranium"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="H._rhodesiensis,_and_the_Gawis_cranium"><i>H. rhodesiensis</i>, and the Gawis cranium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: H. rhodesiensis, and the Gawis cranium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><i>H. rhodesiensis</i>, estimated to be 300,000&#8211;125,000 years old. Most current researchers place Rhodesian Man within the group of <i>Homo heidelbergensis</i>, though other designations such as archaic <i>Homo sapiens</i> and <i>Homo sapiens rhodesiensis</i> have been proposed.</li>\n<li>In February 2006 a fossil, the <a href="/wiki/Gawis_cranium" title="Gawis cranium">Gawis cranium</a>, was found which might possibly be a species intermediate between <i>H. erectus</i> and <i>H. sapiens</i> or one of many evolutionary dead ends. The skull from Gawis, Ethiopia, is believed to be 500,000&#8211;250,000 years old. Only summary details are known, and the finders have not yet released a peer-reviewed study. Gawis man\'s facial features suggest its being either an intermediate species or an example of a "Bodo man" female.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neanderthal_and_Denisovan">Neanderthal and Denisovan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Neanderthal and Denisovan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Neanderthal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denisovan" title="Denisovan">Denisovan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Homo_heidelbergensis_(10233446).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="643" data-file-width="529" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg/170px-Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg/255px-Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg/340px-Homo_heidelbergensis_%2810233446%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Homo_heidelbergensis_(10233446).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">Homo heidelbergensis</a></i> which may be the direct ancestor of both <i><a href="/wiki/Neanderthal" title="Neanderthal">Homo neanderthalensis</a></i> and <i>Homo sapiens</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p><i>Homo neanderthalensis</i>, alternatively designated as <i>Homo sapiens neanderthalensis</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> lived in Europe and Asia from 400,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> to about 28,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>\nThere are a number of clear anatomical differences between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">anatomically modern humans</a> (AMH) and Neanderthal populations. Many of these relate to the superior adaptation to cold environments possessed by the Neanderthal populations. Their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_to_volume_ratio" title="Surface to volume ratio">surface to volume ratio</a> is an extreme version of that found amongst <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">Inuit</a> populations, indicating that they were less inclined to lose body heat than were AMH. From brain Endocasts, Neanderthals also had significantly larger brains. This would seem to indicate that the intellectual superiority of AMH populations may be questionable. More recent research by Eiluned Pearce, Chris Stringer, R.I.M. Dunbar, however, have shown important differences in Brain architecture. For example, in both the orbital chamber size and in the size of the <a href="/wiki/Occipital_lobe" title="Occipital lobe">occipital lobe</a>, the larger size suggests that the Neanderthal had a better visual acuity than modern humans.  This would give a superior vision in the inferior light conditions found in Glacial Europe. It also seems that the higher body mass of Neanderthals had a correspondingly larger brain mass required for body care and control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dunbar_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-dunbar-173">[173]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Neanderthal populations seem to have been physically superior to AMH populations. These differences may have been sufficient to give Neanderthal populations an environmental superiority to AMH populations from 75,000 to 45,000 years BP. With these differences, Neanderthal brains show a smaller area was available for social functioning. Plotting group size possible from endocrainial volume, suggests that AMH populations (minus occipital lobe size), had a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dunbars_number" title="Dunbars number">Dunbars number</a> of 144 possible relationships. Neanderthal populations seem to have been limited to about 120 individuals. This would show up in a larger number of possible mates for AMH humans, with increased risks of inbreeding amongst Neanderthal populations. It also suggests that humans had larger trade catchment areas than Neanderthals (confirmed in the distribution of stone tools). With larger populations, social and technological innovations were easier to fix in human populations, which may have all contributed to the fact that modern Homo sapiens replaced the Neanderthal populations by 28,000 BP.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dunbar_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-dunbar-173">[173]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neandertaler_reconst.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Neandertaler_reconst.jpg/170px-Neandertaler_reconst.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Neandertaler_reconst.jpg/255px-Neandertaler_reconst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Neandertaler_reconst.jpg/340px-Neandertaler_reconst.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neandertaler_reconst.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dermoplastic reconstruction of a Neanderthal</div></div></div>\n<p>Earlier evidence from sequencing mitochondrial DNA suggested that no significant gene flow occurred between <i>H. neanderthalensis</i> and <i>H. sapiens</i>, and that the two were separate species that shared a common ancestor about 660,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> However, a sequencing of the Neanderthal genome in 2010 indicated that Neanderthals did indeed interbreed with anatomically modern humans <i>circa</i> 45,000 to 80,000 years ago (at the approximate time that modern humans migrated out from Africa, but before they dispersed into Europe, Asia and elsewhere).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neandersequence_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-neandersequence-177">[177]</a></sup>  The genetic sequencing of a 40,000 year old <a href="/wiki/Pe%C8%99tera_cu_Oase" title="Pe&#537;tera cu Oase">human skeleton from Romania</a> showed that 11% of its genome was Neanderthal, and it was estimated that the individual had a Neanderthal ancestor 4&#8211;6 generations previously,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> in addition to a contribution from earlier interbreeding in the Middle East.  Though this interbred Romanian population seems not to have been ancestral to modern humans, the finding indicates that interbreeding happened repeatedly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nearly all modern non-African humans have 1% to 4% of their DNA derived from Neanderthal DNA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neandersequence_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-neandersequence-177">[177]</a></sup> and this finding is consistent with recent studies indicating that the divergence of some human alleles dates to one Ma, although the interpretation of these studies has been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> Neanderthals and <i>Homo sapiens</i> could have co-existed in Europe for as long as 10,000 years, during which human populations exploded vastly outnumbering Neanderthals, possibly outcompeting them by sheer numerical strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2008, archaeologists working at the site of <a href="/wiki/Denisova_Cave" title="Denisova Cave">Denisova Cave</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Mountains" title="Altai Mountains">Altai Mountains</a> of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> uncovered a small bone fragment from the fifth finger of a juvenile member of Denisovans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> Artifacts, including a bracelet, excavated in the cave at the same level were <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating" title="Radiocarbon dating">carbon dated</a> to around 40,000 BP. As DNA had survived in the fossil fragment due to the cool climate of the Denisova Cave, both mtDNA and nuclear DNA were sequenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21179161_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21179161-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P&#228;&#228;bo_et_al._184-0"><a href="#cite_note-P&#228;&#228;bo_et_al.-184">[184]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the divergence point of the mtDNA was unexpectedly deep in time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Scientist_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Scientist-185">[185]</a></sup> the full genomic sequence suggested the Denisovans belonged to the same lineage as Neanderthals, with the two diverging shortly after their line split from the lineage that gave rise to modern humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21179161_90-4"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21179161-90">[90]</a></sup> Modern humans are known to have overlapped with Neanderthals in Europe and the Near East for possibly more than 40,000 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> and the discovery raises the possibility that Neanderthals, Denisovans, and modern humans may have co-existed and interbred. The existence of this distant branch creates a much more complex picture of humankind during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Pleistocene" title="Late Pleistocene">Late Pleistocene</a> than previously thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P&#228;&#228;bo_et_al._184-1"><a href="#cite_note-P&#228;&#228;bo_et_al.-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> Evidence has also been found that as much as 6% of the DNA of some modern <a href="/wiki/Melanesians" title="Melanesians">Melanesians</a> derive from Denisovans, indicating limited interbreeding in Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reich_2011_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reich_2011-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Alleles thought to have originated in Neanderthals and Denisovans have been identified at several genetic loci in the genomes of modern humans outside of Africa. HLA haplotypes from Denisovans and Neanderthal represent more than half the HLA alleles of modern Eurasians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10.1126/science.1209202_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-10.1126/science.1209202-92">[92]</a></sup> indicating strong positive selection for these <a href="/wiki/Introgression" title="Introgression">introgressed</a> alleles.  Corinne Simoneti at Vanderbilt University, in Nashville and her team have found from medical records of 28,000 people of European descent that the presence of Neanderthal DNA segments may be associated with a likelihood to suffer depression more frequently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The flow of genes from Neanderthal populations to modern human was not all one way.  Sergi Castellano of the Max Planck Institute for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolutionary_Anthropology" title="Evolutionary Anthropology">Evolutionary Anthropology</a> in Leipzig, Germany, has in 2016 reported that while Denisovan and Neanderthal genomes are more related to each other than they are to us, Siberian Neanderthal genomes show similarity to the modern human gene pool, more so than to European Neanderthal populations.  The evidence suggests that the Neanderthal populations interbred with modern humans possibly 100,000 years ago, probably somewhere in the Near East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Studies of a Neanderthal child at Gibraltar show from brain development and teeth eruption that Neanderthal children may have matured more rapidly than is the case for Homo sapiens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="H._floresiensis"><i>H. floresiensis</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: H. floresiensis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Homo_floresiensis" title="Homo floresiensis">Homo floresiensis</a></div>\n<p><i>H. floresiensis</i>, which lived from approximately 190,000 to 50,000 years <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_present" title="Before present">before present</a> (BP), has been nicknamed <i><a href="/wiki/Hobbit" title="Hobbit">hobbit</a></i> for its small size, possibly a result of <a href="/wiki/Insular_dwarfism" title="Insular dwarfism">insular dwarfism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> <i>H. floresiensis</i> is intriguing both for its size and its age, being an example of a recent species of the genus <i>Homo</i> that exhibits derived traits not shared with modern humans. In other words, <i>H. floresiensis</i> shares a common ancestor with modern humans, but split from the modern human lineage and followed a distinct evolutionary path. The main find was a skeleton believed to be a woman of about 30 years of age. Found in 2003, it has been dated to approximately 18,000 years old. The living woman was estimated to be one meter in height, with a brain volume of just 380&#160;cm<sup>3</sup> (considered small for a chimpanzee and less than a third of the <i>H. sapiens</i> average of 1400&#160;cm<sup>3</sup>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources.
