(April 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>However, there is an ongoing debate over whether <i>H. floresiensis</i> is indeed a separate species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> Some scientists hold that <i>H. floresiensis</i> was a modern <i>H. sapiens</i> with pathological dwarfism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-194">[194]</a></sup> This hypothesis is supported in part, because some modern humans who live on <a href="/wiki/Flores" title="Flores">Flores</a>, the Indonesian island where the skeleton was found, are <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_peoples#Rampasasa" title="Pygmy peoples">pygmies</a>. This, coupled with pathological dwarfism, could have resulted in a significantly diminutive human. The other major attack on <i>H. floresiensis</i> as a separate species is that it was found with tools only associated with <i>H. sapiens</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-Martin-194">[194]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The hypothesis of pathological dwarfism, however, fails to explain <a href="/wiki/Homo_floresiensis#Additional_features" title="Homo floresiensis">additional anatomical features</a> that are unlike those of modern humans (diseased or not) but much like those of ancient members of our genus. Aside from cranial features, these features include the form of bones in the wrist, forearm, shoulder, knees, and feet. Additionally, this hypothesis fails to explain the find of multiple examples of individuals with these same characteristics, indicating they were common to a large population, and not limited to one individual.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="H._sapiens"><i>H. sapiens</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: H. sapiens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Archaic_humans" title="Archaic humans">Archaic humans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans" title="Anatomically modern humans">Anatomically modern humans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">Archaic human admixture with modern humans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens#Recent_evolution" title="Homo sapiens">Homo sapiens &#167;&#160;Recent evolution</a></div>\n<p><i>H. sapiens</i> (the adjective <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sapiens" title="wikt:sapiens">sapiens</a></i> is Latin for "wise" or "intelligent") emerged around 300,000 years ago, likely derived from <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_heidelbergensis" title="Homo heidelbergensis">Homo heidelbergensis</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schlebusch2017_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schlebusch2017-195">[195]</a></sup> Between 400,000 years ago and the second interglacial period in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Pleistocene" title="Middle Pleistocene">Middle Pleistocene</a>, around 250,000 years ago, the trend in <a href="/wiki/Brain_size#Cranial_capacity" title="Brain size">intra-cranial volume expansion</a> and the elaboration of stone tool technologies developed, providing evidence for a transition from <i>H. erectus</i> to <i>H. sapiens</i>. The direct evidence suggests there was a migration of <i>H. erectus</i> out of Africa, then a further <a href="/wiki/Speciation" title="Speciation">speciation</a> of <i>H. sapiens</i> from <i>H. erectus</i> in Africa. A subsequent migration (both within and out of Africa) eventually replaced the earlier dispersed <i>H. erectus</i>. This migration and origin theory is usually referred to as the "recent single-origin hypothesis" or "out of Africa" theory.\n<i>H. sapiens</i> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">interbred with archaic humans</a> both in Africa and in Eurasia, in Eurasia notably with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neanderthals" title="Neanderthals">Neanderthals</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denisovans" title="Denisovans">Denisovans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid21179161_90-5"><a href="#cite_note-pmid21179161-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reich_2011_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-Reich_2011-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory" title="Toba catastrophe theory">Toba catastrophe theory</a>, which postulates a <a href="/wiki/Population_bottleneck" title="Population bottleneck">population bottleneck</a> for <i>H. sapiens</i> about 70,000 years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ambrose1998_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-ambrose1998-196">[196]</a></sup> was controversial from its first proposal in the 1990s and by the 2010s had very little support.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>\nDistinctive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_genetic_variability" title="Human genetic variability">human genetic variability</a> has arisen as the result of the <a href="/wiki/Founder_effect" title="Founder effect">founder effect</a>, by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans" title="Archaic human admixture with modern humans">archaic admixture</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Recent_human_evolution" title="Recent human evolution">recent evolutionary pressures</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_tools">Use of tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Use of tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8225" data-file-width="9912" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg/220px-Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg/330px-Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg/440px-Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"A sharp rock", an <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a> pebble tool, the most basic of human stone tools.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Small_bonfire.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Small_bonfire.JPG/220px-Small_bonfire.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Small_bonfire.JPG/330px-Small_bonfire.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Small_bonfire.JPG/440px-Small_bonfire.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Small_bonfire.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The harnessing of fire was a pivotal milestone in human history.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1464" data-file-width="896" decoding="async" height="278" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg/170px-Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg/255px-Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg/340px-Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">Acheulean</a> hand-axes from <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a>. <i>Homo erectus</i> <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a> work. The types shown are (clockwise from top) cordate, ficron and ovate.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2220" data-file-width="1350" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg/170px-Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg/255px-Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg/340px-Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Venus_of_Willendorf" title="Venus of Willendorf">Venus of Willendorf</a>, an example of <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a> art, dated 24&#8211;26,000 years ago.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Hunting_hypothesis" title="Hunting hypothesis">Hunting hypothesis</a></div>\n<p>The use of tools has been interpreted as a sign of intelligence, and it has been theorized that tool use may have stimulated certain aspects of human evolution, especially the continued expansion of the human brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-198">[198]</a></sup> Paleontology has yet to explain the expansion of this organ over millions of years despite being extremely demanding in terms of energy consumption. The brain of a modern human consumes about 13 <a href="/wiki/Watt" title="Watt">watts</a> (260 kilocalories per day), a fifth of the body\'s resting power consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> Increased tool use would allow hunting for energy-rich meat products, and would enable processing more energy-rich plant products. Researchers have suggested that early hominins were thus under evolutionary pressure to increase their capacity to create and use tools.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Precisely when early humans started to use tools is difficult to determine, because the more primitive these tools are (for example, sharp-edged stones) the more difficult it is to decide whether they are natural objects or human artifacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-198">[198]</a></sup> There is some evidence that the australopithecines (4 Ma) <a href="/wiki/Osteodontokeratic_culture" title="Osteodontokeratic culture">may have used broken bones as tools</a>, but this is debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals" title="Tool use by animals">Many species make and use tools</a>, but it is the human genus that dominates the areas of making and using more complex tools. The oldest known tools are flakes from West Turkana, Kenya, which date to 3.3 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmand_310&#8211;315_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harmand_310&#8211;315-202">[202]</a></sup> The next oldest stone tools are from Gona, Ethiopia, and are considered the beginning of the Oldowan technology. These tools date to about 2.6 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> A <i>Homo</i> fossil was found near some <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan tools</a>, and its age was noted at 2.3 million years old, suggesting that maybe the <i>Homo</i> species did indeed create and use these tools. It is a possibility but does not yet represent solid evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-204">[204]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Third_metacarpal_styloid_process" title="Third metacarpal styloid process">third metacarpal styloid process</a> enables the hand bone to lock into the wrist bones, allowing for greater amounts of pressure to be applied to the wrist and hand from a grasping thumb and fingers. It allows humans the dexterity and strength to make and use complex tools. This unique anatomical feature separates humans from apes and other nonhuman primates, and is not seen in human fossils older than 1.8 million years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bernard Wood noted that <i>Paranthropus</i> co-existed with the early <i>Homo</i> species in the area of the "Oldowan Industrial Complex" over roughly the same span of time. Although there is no direct evidence which identifies <i>Paranthropus</i> as the tool makers, their anatomy lends to indirect evidence of their capabilities in this area. Most paleoanthropologists agree that the early <i>Homo</i> species were indeed responsible for most of the Oldowan tools found. They argue that when most of the Oldowan tools were found in association with human fossils, <i>Homo</i> was always present, but <i>Paranthropus</i> was not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_204-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-204">[204]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1994, Randall Susman used the anatomy of opposable thumbs as the basis for his argument that both the <i>Homo</i> and <i>Paranthropus</i> species were toolmakers. He compared bones and muscles of human and chimpanzee thumbs, finding that humans have 3 muscles which are lacking in chimpanzees. Humans also have thicker metacarpals with broader heads, allowing more precise grasping than the chimpanzee hand can perform. Susman posited that modern anatomy of the human opposable thumb is an evolutionary response to the requirements associated with making and handling tools and that both species were indeed toolmakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_204-2"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-204">[204]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stone_tools">Stone tools</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Stone tools">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Stone_tool" title="Stone tool">Stone tool</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Stone_tool" title="Stone tool">Stone tools</a> are first attested around 2.6 Million years ago, when hominins in Eastern Africa used so-called core <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">tools</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chopper_(archaeology)" title="Chopper (archaeology)">choppers</a> made out of round cores that had been split by simple strikes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plummer_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plummer-206">[206]</a></sup> This marks the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>, or Old <a href="/wiki/Stone_Age" title="Stone Age">Stone Age</a>; its end is taken to be the end of the last <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">Ice Age</a>, around 10,000 years ago. The Paleolithic is subdivided into the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Paleolithic" title="Lower Paleolithic">Lower Paleolithic</a> (Early Stone Age), ending around 350,000&#8211;300,000 years ago, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic" title="Middle Paleolithic">Middle Paleolithic</a> (Middle Stone Age), until 50,000&#8211;30,000 years ago, and the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic" title="Upper Paleolithic">Upper Paleolithic</a>, (Late Stone Age), 50,000&#8211;10,000 years ago.\n</p><p>Archaeologists working in the Great Rift Valley in Kenya have discovered the oldest known stone tools in the world. Dated to around 3.3 million years ago, the implements are some 700,000 years older than stone tools from Ethiopia that previously held this distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmand_310&#8211;315_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harmand_310&#8211;315-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The period from 700,000&#8211;300,000 years ago is also known as the <a href="/wiki/Acheulean" title="Acheulean">Acheulean</a>, when <i>H. ergaster</i> (or <i>erectus</i>) made large stone <a href="/wiki/Hand_axe" title="Hand axe">hand axes</a> out of <a href="/wiki/Flint" title="Flint">flint</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quartzite" title="Quartzite">quartzite</a>, at first quite rough (Early Acheulian), later "<a href="/wiki/Retouch_(lithics)" title="Retouch (lithics)">retouched</a>" by additional, more-subtle strikes at the sides of the <a href="/wiki/Lithic_flake" title="Lithic flake">flakes</a>. After 350,000 BP the more refined so-called <a href="/wiki/Levallois_technique" title="Levallois technique">Levallois technique</a> was developed, a series of consecutive strikes, by which scrapers, slicers ("racloirs"), needles, and flattened needles were made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plummer_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-Plummer-206">[206]</a></sup> Finally, after about 50,000 BP, ever more refined and specialized flint tools were made by the Neanderthals and the immigrant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cro-Magnon" title="Cro-Magnon">Cro-Magnons</a> (knives, blades, skimmers). In this period they also started to make tools out of bone.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transition_to_behavioral_modernity">Transition to behavioral modernity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Transition to behavioral modernity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Behavioral_modernity" title="Behavioral modernity">Behavioral modernity</a></div>\n<p>Until about 50,000&#8211;40,000 years ago, the use of stone tools seems to have progressed stepwise. Each phase (<i>H. habilis</i>, <i>H. ergaster</i>, <i>H. neanderthalensis</i>) started at a higher level than the previous one, but after each phase started, further development was slow. Currently paleoanthropologists are debating whether these <i>Homo</i> species possessed some or many of the cultural and behavioral traits associated with modern humans such as language, complex symbolic thinking, technological creativity etc. It seems that they were culturally conservative maintaining simple technologies and foraging patterns over very long periods.\n</p><p>Around 50,000 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>, modern human culture started to evolve more rapidly. The transition to behavioral modernity has been characterized by most as a Eurasian "Great Leap Forward",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup> or as the "Upper Palaeolithic Revolution",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup> due to the sudden appearance of distinctive signs of modern behavior and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_game_hunting" title="Big game hunting">big game hunting</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oppenheimer_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-oppenheimer-97">[97]</a></sup> in the archaeological record. Some other scholars consider the transition to have been more gradual, noting that some features had already appeared among archaic African <i>Homo sapiens</i> since 200,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup>  Recent evidence suggests that the Australian Aboriginal population separated from the African population 75,000 years ago, and that they made a sea journey of up to 160&#160;km 60,000 years ago, which may diminish the evidence of the Upper Paleolithic Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Modern humans started burying their dead, using animal hides to make clothing, hunting with more sophisticated techniques (such as using <a href="/wiki/Trapping_pit" title="Trapping pit">trapping pits</a> or driving animals off cliffs), and engaging in <a href="/wiki/Cave_painting" title="Cave painting">cave painting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> As human culture advanced, different populations of humans introduced novelty to existing technologies: artifacts such as fish hooks, buttons, and bone needles show signs of variation among different populations of humans, something that had not been seen in human cultures prior to 50,000 BP. Typically, <i>H. neanderthalensis</i> populations do not vary in their technologies, although the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chatelperronian" title="Chatelperronian">Chatelperronian</a> assemblages have been found to be Neanderthal innovations produced as a result of exposure to the Homo sapiens <a href="/wiki/Aurignacian" title="Aurignacian">Aurignacian</a> technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Among concrete examples of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_human_behavior" title="Modern human behavior">modern human behavior</a>, anthropologists include specialization of tools, use of jewellery and images (such as cave drawings), organization of living space, rituals (for example, burials with grave gifts), specialized hunting techniques, exploration of less hospitable geographical areas, and <a href="/wiki/Barter" title="Barter">barter</a> trade networks. Debate continues as to whether a "revolution" led to modern humans ("the big bang of human consciousness"), or whether the evolution was more "gradual".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mcbrearty_Brooks_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mcbrearty_Brooks-146">[146]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_and_current_human_evolution">Recent and current human evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Recent and current human evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recent_human_evolution" title="Recent human evolution">Recent human evolution</a></div>\n<p>Evolution has continued in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatomically_modern_human" title="Anatomically modern human">anatomically modern human</a> populations, which are affected by both natural selection and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_drift" title="Genetic drift">genetic drift</a>. Although <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selection_pressure" title="Selection pressure">selection pressure</a> on some traits, such as resistance to smallpox, has decreased in modern human life, humans are still undergoing natural selection for many other traits. Some of these are due to specific environmental pressures, while others are related to lifestyle changes since the development of agriculture (10,000 years ago), urban civilization (5,000), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">industrialization</a> (250 years ago). It has been argued that human evolution has accelerated since the development of agriculture 10,000 years ago and civilization some 5,000 years ago, resulting, it is claimed, in substantial genetic differences between different current human populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Particularly conspicuous is variation in superficial characteristics, such as <a href="/wiki/Afro-textured_hair" title="Afro-textured hair">Afro-textured hair</a>, or the recent evolution of <a href="/wiki/Light_skin" title="Light skin">light skin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blond" title="Blond">blond</a> hair in some populations, which are attributed to differences in climate. Particularly strong selective pressures have resulted in <a href="/wiki/High-altitude_adaptation_in_humans" title="High-altitude adaptation in humans">high-altitude adaptation in humans</a>, with different ones in different isolated populations. Studies of the <a href="/wiki/High-altitude_adaptation_in_humans#Genetic_basis" title="High-altitude adaptation in humans">genetic basis</a> show that some developed very recently, with Tibetans evolving over 3,000 years to have high proportions of an allele of <a href="/wiki/EPAS1" title="EPAS1">EPAS1</a> that is adaptive to high altitudes.\n</p><p>Other evolution is related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic_disease" title="Endemic disease">endemic diseases</a>: the presence of <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> selected for <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_trait" title="Sickle cell trait">sickle cell trait</a> (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterozygote" title="Heterozygote">heterozygote</a> form of sickle cell gene), while the absence of malaria and the health effects of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_anemia" title="Sickle-cell anemia">sickle-cell anemia</a> select against this trait. For example, the population at risk of the severe debilitating disease <a href="/wiki/Kuru_(disease)" title="Kuru (disease)">kuru</a> has significant over-representation of an immune variant of the <a href="/wiki/PRNP" title="PRNP">prion protein</a> gene G127V versus non-immune alleles. The frequency of this <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">genetic variant</a> is due to the survival of immune persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Recent human evolution related to agriculture includes genetic resistance to infectious disease that has appeared in human populations by crossing the species barrier from domesticated animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> as well as changes in metabolism due to changes in diet, such as <a href="/wiki/Lactase_persistence" title="Lactase persistence">lactase persistence</a>.\n</p><p>In contemporary times, since industrialization, some trends have been observed: for instance, menopause is evolving to occur later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1073/pnas.0906199106_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1073/pnas.0906199106-221">[221]</a></sup> Other reported trends appear to include lengthening of the human reproductive period and reduction in cholesterol levels, blood glucose and blood pressure in some populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1073/pnas.0906199106_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1073/pnas.0906199106-221">[221]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Species_list">Species list</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Species list">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<dl><dd><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Template:Homo" title="Template:Homo">List of <i>Homo</i> species</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_evolution/Species_chart" title="Human evolution/Species chart">Human evolution/Species chart</a></i></dd></dl>\n<p>This list is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronological" title="Chronological">chronological</a> order across the table by <i><b><a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a></b></i>.  Some species/subspecies names are well-established, and some are less established &#8211; especially in genus <i>Homo</i>.  Please see articles for more information.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: left; width: 600px; height: 800px; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto; border: boisei</a></i></td>\n<td>&#8226; <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H._s._sapiens" title="H. s. sapiens">H. s. sapiens</a></i> (modern)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus_robustus" title="Paranthropus robustus">P. robustus</a></i></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Adaptive_evolution_in_the_human_genome" title="Adaptive evolution in the human genome">Adaptive evolution in the human genome</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Amity-enmity_complex" title="Amity-enmity complex">Amity-enmity complex</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Archaeogenetics" title="Archaeogenetics">Archaeogenetics</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect"
